16. WASTE MANAGEMENT PLAN FRAMEWORK
 PURPOSE AND APPLICABILITY
 This Waste Management Framework sets out a set of generic requirements for Non-hazardous
 waste management during the construction and operation phases of the Project. It provides general
 guidelines, principles, procedures, steps and Good International Industry Practices (GIIP) that can
 be adapted to the project-specific waste management plan as part of the overarching Environmental
 and Social Management Plan (ESMP) and other relevant environmental and social risk due
 diligence documents of the project. Accordingly, the objectives of this waste management
 framework include but not limited to establishing general requirements for waste management to be
 implemented by the project proponent and contractor to preparing contractor’s waste management
 plan in a manner that is consistent with WB ESF risk mitigation hierarchy and GIIP during the project
 construction and operation.
 During the construction and operations phases of the project, a project onsite -specific waste
 management plan using this general waste management framework, considering project-specific
 situation and waste generation, should be developed as part of the project’s Contractors ESMP or
 other environmental and social documents as appropriate. In addition, during the implementation of
 the project, this waste management framework (or the project contractor specific WMP) will be
 incorporated into the bidding documents and latterly the construction and operation contracts, as
 part of the environmental and social management specifications.
 SCOPE: This Waste Management Framework is a reference guide for contractors to prepare,
 implement and manage waste at their construction sites through sharing some good practices. This
 framework is not intended to be a duplication of the nor a substitute to Contractors Waste
 management plan. However, this waste management framework does not apply to hazardous
 wastes handling for which a separate plan has been prepared. This waste management framework
 should be read in conjunction with the following documents/reports:
        Hazardous Waste Management Plan
        Occupational Health and Safety Management Plans prepared by Contractors to meet
         requirements of Rogun JSC’s OHS Management Plan Framework
        Environmental and Social Management Plan (ESMP) (Volume 3, A1)
        On-Site Traffic Management Plan (Volume 3, A9a)
        Security Management Plan (Volume 3, A7)
 Each Contractor employed at Rogun Hydropower Project (the Project) will prepare and implement a
 Hazardous and Non-hazardous Waste Management Plan (WMP, or Plan) that is consistent with this
 Framework. The Plan will be reviewed by the Employer’s Representative (ER) and/or the Project
 Management Consultant (ER/PMC) and once approved it must be diligently implemented. New
 Contractors must have an approved Plan before mobilizing to site and generating waste, existing
 Contractors within six months of the date that World Bank financing is provided to the Project.
 Clearly , the primary objectives of this Waste Management Plan are:
 To identify and categorize the types of waste generated during dam construction.
 To implement waste reduction, reuse, and recycling measures.



 Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
 Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                  October 2025
 Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                             Page | 43
To ensure the safe and environmentally sound disposal of non-recyclable waste.
To comply with local, national, and international waste management regulations.
To promote awareness and responsibility among all stakeholders involved in the construction and
operation of Rogun HPP.
APPLICABLE STANDARDS
The Plan must include such requirements that will ensure the Project meets the requirements of
Tajikistan law, standards promulgated by International Financial Institutions, the international
obligations of Tajikistan, and good international industry practice (GIIP).
Such requirements will include, at a minimum, the requirements listed in this Framework.
In all cases, site-specific engineering and scientific judgment will be used to confirm that standards
are sufficient to prevent unacceptable impacts on the people and environment. If there are
inconsistencies among or between requirements, the Plan must adopt the more stringent unless that
requirement conflicts with national law, in which case national law applies.
Tajikistan Legislation
       Law of the Republic of Tajikistan “About Production Wastes and Consumption” (Decree 44,
        2002, as amended).
       Law of the Republic of Tajikistan “On Providing Sanitary-Epidemiological Safety of the
        Population” (Decree 49, 2003).
       Law of the Republic of Tajikistan "On Technical Regulation" (no. 522, 2009)
       Decree of the Government of the Republic of Tajikistan "On approval of the Procedure,
        conditions and methods of collection, use, disinfection, transportation, storage and disposal
        of industrial and household waste in the Republic of Tajikistan" (no. 279, 2011).
       Procedure for identifying and accounting for orphan waste (no. 626, 2011).
       Rules for the provision of services for the removal of solid and liquid household waste (no.
        209, 2005).
International Financial Institutions Standards
Various international financial institutions are providing financing for the construction and operation
of Rogun HPP. Each of these institutions requires that projects comply with not only national
legislation but also the institution’s own standards. The various standards that are relevant to waste
generation and management are exemplified by World Bank ESS3.
Paragraphs 17 to 23 of the ESS 3 (Resource Efficiency and Pollution Prevention and Management)
outline the following principles for managing non-hazardous and hazardous waste:
       Minimize waste generation to the extent feasible.
       Reuse, recycle and recover waste to the extent possible, in a manner that is safe for human
        health and the environment. Where waste cannot be reused, recycled, or recovered, it must
        be treated, destroyed, and/or disposed of it in an environmentally sound and safe manner.
       Where hazardous waste is generated, the Contractor will comply with national waste laws
        and applicable international conventions, including those relating to transboundary
        movement of waste. Where such requirements are absent, the Project will adopt Good




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 44
        International Industry Practice (GIIP) alternatives for its environmentally sound and safe
        management and disposal.
       Verify that hazardous waste management contractors are reputable and legitimate
        companies licensed by the relevant government authority, with respect to transportation and
        disposal. Waste manifest documentation to the final disposal destination must be obtained
        and kept on file.
       Ensure that sites to which waste is disposed are properly licensed and are being operated to
        acceptable standards.
International Obligations
International Conventions and Protocols that apply include, but are not limited to:
       Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs).
       Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer (Montreal protocol),
        reflected in Order No. 477 of the Government of the Republic of Tajikistan (December 3,
        2002), “About measures for accomplishment of the Vienna convention on protection of
        ozone layer and the Montreal Protocol for the substances destroying ozone layer.”
       Vienna Convention for the Protection of the Ozone Layer – ratified by Tajikistan in 1996.
       Convention on International Prior Informed Consent Procedure for Certain Trade in
        Hazardous Chemicals and Pesticides in International Trade Rotterdam (PIC)—not in force in
        Tajikistan, which has signed but not ratified the Convention.
       Basel Convention on the Control of Transboundary Movements of Hazardous Wastes and
        Their Disposal—ratified by Tajikistan in 2016.
Good International Industry Practice
       World Bank Group Environmental, Health and Safety (EHS) General Guidelines:
        Environmental, section 1 1.6 “Waste Management”.
MANAGEMENT APPROACH AND ACTIVITIES
Potential Environmental and Social Impacts
The following are the potential environmental risks that might be considered associated with the
improper management of waste, including segregation, labelling, temporary storage, evacuation
and final disposal into landfills:
       Generation of leachate and the release of pollutants and heavy metals to the environment
        due to unsafe and improper disposal of generated waste, posing health and safety risks to
        the public.
       Contamination soil, affecting soil fertility and agricultural yield.
       Water, air, and soil pollution due to the release of environmental pollutants.
       There would be risk in the scavenging acts such as scavenging resaleable materials.
       In addition, improper collection, management, and disposal of waste could pose the
        following community health and social risks.
       Nuisance to communities due to aesthetical and visual pollution
       Groundwater resource contamination.
       Various health impacts in water, air, and soil.



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                    October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                               Page | 45
The WMP will be based on a detailed evaluation of ongoing and new operations. The Plan will be
informed by and based upon the best judgment of qualified professionals. The Plan will become part
of the Contractor’s Environmental and Social Management and Monitoring System.
The best way to manage waste is to avoid the generation of waste. The overarching principle of the
WMPs should be the application of the waste management hierarchy: prevention, reduction, reuse,
recycle, treatment, and disposal. The WMP will be based on a comprehensive waste inventory,
which should include detailed information on all waste streams, including:
       Name and description of the waste.
       Classification of the waste as per European Union List of Waste (Commission Decision
        2000/532/EC) or equivalent system.
       The source, physical and chemical characteristics, and the amount of waste, and whether it
        is to be managed as nonhazardous or hazardous waste.
       At all stages from initial generation to ultimate disposal (that is, from “cradle to grave”), the
        means by which the waste is managed, including recycling, reuse, transfer, temporary and
        long-term storage, handling, and disposal.
       The locations where generated waste is stored, used, or otherwise managed.
       Details on containment, storage, transfer, transport, and disposal (or reuse or recycle),
        including procedures for verifying contract waste handlers or haulers and disposal sites are
        properly licensed and in compliance with Tajik legislation.
       Any other information required by Tajikistan law and the World Bank ESF/ESS3.
For all new Contractors, the Plan should take a systematic approach to identify and evaluate ways
to:
       Design or modify processes to avoid waste generation;
       Design or modify processes and practices to reduce waste generation;
       Design activities to maximize the reuse of waste and surplus materials; and
       Design operations so that waste is recovered and/or recycled as much as possible.
For existing Contractors, the WMP will include a timebound process by which this approach to
ongoing waste generation will be implemented.
It is noted that disposal should be a last resort, used only when avoidance, reduction, reuse, and
recycling/recovery are not economically and technically feasible. If practicable, waste should be
treated if that is needed to make them reusable, recoverable, or recyclable, and to ensure that
disposal cannot lead to unacceptable risks to human health or the environment.
Typical waste streams that may be generated by Contractors at Rogun HPP would include, but not
be limited to:
Nonhazardous waste could include, but not be limited to, the following:
       Sediment from settling ponds used to store and “treat” precipitation run-off;
       Treatment residues and sludges from water and wastewater treatment;
       Household waste and garbage from accommodations, construction camps, offices, and
        workplaces during construction and operation;
       Wrecked or worn-out vehicles, equipment, machinery;


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                  Page | 46
       Demolition debris from buildings, foundation, and other features that are removed during
        construction or operation (except asbestos, which must be managed in accordance with the
        Demolition (Asbestos) Management Plan);
       Construction debris, including wood, metal, plastic, concrete and other inert solid materials;
       Ash from combustion of coal or other fuels, and/or from incineration of waste and other
        materials; and
       Spoil from tunnelling, excavations, and grading land surfaces. If substantial amounts of non-
        reactive earthen waste (e.g., tunnelling or excavation spoil) may be generated by a project,
        a separate management plan for such materials may be appropriate since management
        methods are likely to be different for earthen and non-earthen waste.
Waste management encompasses the functions of collection, transfer, treatment, recycling,
resource recovery and disposal of municipal solid waste. However, waste management and disposal
issues can be grouped into three distinct categories that require different disposal management
methods. Each has its unique characterization, scale and particular issues. The three categories:
Medical and Healthcare Waste; Municipal Waste and Hazardous Waste
Plans must describe how waste streams will be segregated and managed to meet applicable
standards for reuse, recycling, storage, evacuation, and/or disposal, and to prevent adverse impacts
on soil , surface water, ground water, air, and other resources and sensitive receptors, including
humans. Plans must also describe the process by which decisions were made regarding waste
management and describe all steps by which waste will be managed in a manner that is consistent
with the ESF, national laws of Tajikistan and GIIP. Plans should describe options that were
considered to reduce and/or recycle waste (and describe why such measures were not adopted if
they were not).
If waste streams are or will be managed off-site, the Plan will require that Contractors and/or
facilities (as appropriate) verify that companies or individuals who will transport and manage the
waste (which can include reuse, recycling, and/or disposal) are properly licensed to engage in those
practices. Similarly, they must verify that off-site treatment and disposal sites to which waste is
transported are properly permitted, designed, and operated to contain the waste being treated or
disposed. These requirements apply even if the waste is sold and thus may not be considered
“waste” per se.
The transportation of waste within and from the Project site must align with site specific protocols
and applicable regulatory standards to ensure safe operations and environmental compliance.
Contractors will be responsible for including appropriate requirements concerning routes, vehicle,
and driver requirements in their Transport Management Plans (TMPs).
Disposal of waste in the Rogun municipal landfill is not permitted as, although it is an official
municipal landfill, it is not an engineered landfill – it is overflowing and there is no material to cover
the waste as it is tipped. The lack of engineered cells and cover material limits the long-term
suitability of the facility to continue as a municipal landfill. The landfill is located above a gully that
connects to the reservoir and, with snowmelt and precipitation, runoff from the landfill flows directly
into the reservoir. Consequently, an actual “plan” for the management of waste that takes into
account the site conditions, and preven runoff and leachate from the landfill to the river should be
developed.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                         October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                    Page | 47
However, the absence of engineered and/or licensed landfill sites in Tajikistan that can manage non-
hazardous (and hazardous waste) in compliance with the standards does not relieve the Contractor
of the obligation to meet the standards, which may require development of compliant waste
management facilities.
Waste management plans must require that local communities are consulted and informed of
hazardous waste that will be transported through or to their communities or might otherwise have an
adverse effect on communities through fire, explosion, spills, or other incidents. This will include
consultations with local first responders and medical facilities to ensure they can make appropriate
preparations for any future requests for support.
Besides the initial inventory, the inventory of nonhazardous and hazardous waste must be updated
on at least a semi-annual basis, whenever a new waste stream is generated, or when there is a
significant change in the quantity of waste generated or the management methods
POTENTIAL MITIGATION MEASURES
The Plan must describe the measures that will be used to manage waste. The selection of
measures should be based first on their effectiveness in avoiding waste generation and the release
of contained waste, and only then with consideration of other factors.
Mitigation measures include:
       Implementation of the waste management hierarchy to minimize the volume of waste
        produced and maximize reuse, recovery, and recycling of materials wherever technically
        and economically feasible;
       Segregation of waste types (e.g., wood, paper, metal, organic, hazardous, etc.);
       Minimum requirements for contractors handling, transporting and disposing of waste include
        a current operating license issued in terms of Tajik regulatory requirements, and the
        capacity in terms of technical and associated resources to follow GIIP;
       Prohibition on the disposal of waste in the Rogun municipal landfill site;
       Storage of neutralizing materials in areas where acidic or alkaline materials are stored and
        used.
       Method statement for the cleaning and/or neutralization of empty containers and how
        residue and the containers themselves will be managed, with residues and containers of
        waste.
       Specification of personal protective equipment (PPE) that will be required (and enforced) for
        all personnel who use or may be exposed to hazardous waste.
       Drainage from the containment area must not be connected to the site runoff system. If
        dedicated containment structures cannot be constructed, then portable drain covers (which
        can be deployed for the duration of the operations), automatic shut-off valves on storm
        water basins, or shut off valves in drainage or sewer facilities, must be installed;
       Workers and drivers must be trained in the handling of waste, especially in confined areas
        such as the tunnels;
       All waste containers designated for off-site shipment must be secured and labelled with the
        contents and be properly loaded on the transport vehicles before leaving the site, and be
        accompanied by a shipping paper (i.e., manifest) that describes the content of the load;
       Establish a waste register to record all waste removal;



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 48
       Ensure that waste management contractors are licensed by the relevant government
        regulatory agencies and that their permits are renewed in time; and
       Waste manifest documentation (including a certificate of safe disposal) must be issued after
        every collection and kept on record by the contractor.
Demolition Waste
       Any Asbestos containing materials (ACMs) must be disposed in accordance with the
        Demolition (Asbestos) Management Plan;
       All reusable materials should be recovered from the buildings to be demolished in
        accordance with the waste management hierarchy;
       The inert demolition material can be used as cover materials on landfills for construction
        waste based on its composition; and
       Assess the feasibility of the creation of one or more on-site repositories that would isolate
        waste and prevent the escape of pollutants into the ground, air or the water sources.
MONITORING, RECORDKEEPING, AND REPORTING
Contractor’s WMP will require that an inventory of all waste streams is kept up to date. The Plan will
be updated as soon as new waste is generated, with an update at least semi-annually (that is, each
six months). The Plan must describe what actions will be taken, and by whom, when, if inspection
results show that the materials and/or waste management practices do not meet applicable
requirements, or if there is evidence that there may have been releases of waste from usage,
storage, and/or disposal activities and sites.
The Plan will include the same requirements for subcontractors or require that subcontractors
implement and comply with the Contractor’s approved WMP. The WMP will require that records be
kept as required by Tajik law, but no less than the term of the Contractor’s works at Rogun HPP.
The Plan will also identify how, and the level within the Contractor, the ER, and Rogun JSC to which
violations of requirements and/or adverse effects due to waste management will be reported. The
Plan must specify requirements for daily and monthly monitoring, including, but not limited to,
activities described below.
Daily onsite observation monitoring and checks/audits which must include:
       Daily inspections of waste segregation, labelling and collection practices;
       Daily visual inspection of all waste storage collection and storage areas for evidence of
        accidental releases and to verify that waste is properly labelled and stored. When significant
        quantities of waste are generated and stored on site, monitoring activities must include:
       Daily inspection of vessels for leaks, drips, or other indications of loss;
       Identification of cracks, corrosion, or damage to tanks, protective equipment, or floors;
       Verification of locks, emergency valves, and other safety devices for easy operation
        (lubricating if required and employing the practice of keeping locks and safety equipment in
        standby position when the area is not occupied);
       Checking the operability of emergency systems;
       Documenting results of testing for integrity, emissions, or monitoring stations (air, soil vapor,
        or groundwater); and




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 49
       Documenting any changes to the waste storage facility, and any significant changes in the
        quantity of waste in storage.
Monthly waste monitoring must be conducted to track and record:
       Types, quantities, and sources of non-hazardous waste streams;
       Log of movements of non-hazardous waste off site including, volumes, destination, and
        courier;
       Status of the capacity of facilities to continue to receive non-hazardous waste from the
        Project;
       Tracking of waste generation trends by type and amount of waste generated, preferably per
        department when feasible, for example, office, workshops, accommodation, etc.;
Monitoring records for waste collected, stored, or shipped must include:
       Name and identification number of the material(s) composing the waste;
       Physical state (i.e., solid, liquid, gaseous or a combination of one, or more, of these);
       Quantity (e.g., kilograms or liters, number of containers);
       Waste shipment tracking documentation to include, quantity and type, date dispatched, date
        transported and date received, record of the originator, the receiver, and the transporter;
       Method and date of storing, repacking, treating, or disposing at the facility, cross-referenced
        to specific manifest document numbers applicable to the hazardous waste; and
       Location of each waste within the facility, and the quantity at each location.
The Plan must require periodic (at least twice per year) inspection/ monitoring by qualified HSE
personnel of all waste storage, transport, and disposal sites and activities, including storage, use,
and disposal areas, containment and segregation locations, transport and unloading operations, off-
site management, etc. The Plan will specify the reports that will be prepared, to whom the reports
are to be submitted for review, and the length of time records will be kept. This will include
summaries in monthly progress reports submitted to the ER. At a minimum, such reports should
include information on the types and quantities of waste, including quantities and types of waste
generated, segregated, labelled, stored, evacuated and disposed of on- or off-site (including details
of haulers and of methods of disposal). In addition, the Contactor will prepare and submit such
reports as are required by the World Bank and or Tajik authorities.
IMPLEMENTATION
The contractor’s WMP will identify and describe the institutional arrangement including roles and
responsibilities of all parties who will implement, supervise, or otherwise have a role in implementing
the requirements of the WMP. This should be shown in organograms that include as much detail as
possible, down to the individual position/person and their titles. The Contractor’s WMP will also
cover its own materials and waste management and that of all subcontractors.
TRAINING
The WMP must identify the training that will be provided to all staff and managers who will be
involved in any way in waste management so they can perform their responsibilities at minimum risk
to themselves, other workers, the Project, and the environment. To this end, contractor’s WMP will
identify who will be responsible for conducting the training and who will be trained. Training will
focus on the assigned responsibilities of the trainees and will cover technical and administrative


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 50
skills needed to perform assigned responsibilities related to materials and waste management, the
actions required in case of incidents, and topics related to occupational health and safety, including
PPE required to conduct assigned activities.
The WMP will require workers, including drivers, to receive specific health and safety training for the
materials they use or could be exposed to, and all drivers and equipment operators must receive
training for fueling and waste spill cleanup. Workers will also receive training for spill response and
cleanup activities.
Only workers who have received the relevant training will be allowed to work with or be exposed to
waste.
RELATIONSHIP TO OTHER PLANS
Other plans that are or may be related to Waste Management Plan include:
       Hazardous Waste Management Plan
       Demolition (Asbestos) Management Plan.
       Community Health and Safety Plan.
       Emergency Preparedness and Response Plan.
       Occupational Health and Safety Plan.
       Spill Prevention and Response Plan.
       Stakeholder Engagement Plan.
       Traffic Management Plan.
       Water Management Plan.
REVISION
The WMP will be reviewed at least semi-annually (that is, twice per year) and updated when:
       There is a significant change in project or site conditions or waste management (e.g., new
        waste stream, change in treatment or storage, change in hauler or disposal site, etc.)
       Any mitigation measure is determined to be insufficient to achieve its purpose.
       There is a major spill or release of a waste.
       It is necessary to improve waste management to ensure compliance with applicable ESF
        standards and GIIP.
CONCLUSION
The construction and operation of the Rogun HPP involves significant activities that may generate
various types of waste. Effective waste management is crucial to minimize environmental impacts,
ensure compliance with regulations, and promote sustainable development. This Waste
Management Framework Plan outlines the principles, procedures, processes, timelines, measures
and steps to safely manage, handle, reduce, reuse, and dispose non-hazardous waste resulting
from the construction and operation of Rogun HPP. The specific aim is to ensure that waste
generated by the are identified, classified and managed through environmentally sound procedures
and practices. In terms of scope, this WMF applies to solid and liquid wastes being generated within.
It covers: (i) Waste Classification and (ii) Segregation, (iii) Waste Collection, (iv) Handling and
Storage; (v) Waste Recording, (vi) Evacuation/Transfer and (vii) Disposal. This WMF applies to all

Project contractors and subcontractors and employees that are involved in generation or
management of waste in the project. Each Contractor will prepare and implement a WMP that is
consistent with this Framework. The Plan will be reviewed and approved by the Contractor’s Project
Manager and submitted to the Employer’s Representative (ER) and/or the Project Management
Consultant (ER/PMC) for review. Once approved, it shall be diligently implemented by the
Contractor. The successful implementation of this Waste Management Plan for the dam construction
HPP will contribute to minimizing environmental impacts, ensuring regulatory compliance, and
promoting sustainable construction practices. By adhering to the strategies and practices outlined in
this plan, all stakeholders can work together to achieve a cleaner, safer, and more sustainable
construction project.

18. PURPOSE AND APPLICABILITY
 1. The purpose of this Framework is to identify the minimum requirements for identifying, preparing for, and
    responding to emergencies that could reasonably be expected to occur at the Rogun Hydropower Project
    (HPP) construction site and to define the requirements of JSC Rogun and Contractors’ EPRPs.
 2. The purpose of the construction EPRP Plan is to ensure that the Contractor has identified, taken action to
    prevent, and is prepared to respond to emergencies that do occur, including having the capacity and ability
    to respond quickly to minimize damages and limiting risks to life and health.
 3. Each Contractor employed at Rogun Hydropower Project (the Project) must have an Emergency
    Preparedness and Response Plan (EPRP) that is consistent with this Framework and has been approved by
    the Contractor’s Project Manager and by the Employer’s Representative (ER) and/or the Project
    Management Consultant (ER/PMC). If an existing Contractor has an EPRP that has been approved by the
    ER, a new plan is not needed if the ER verifies the existing plan is equivalent to what is required by this
    Framework, or if the Contractor makes sufficient changes to the existing plan that the ER determines it is
    equivalent. Once approved by the ER/PMC, it shall be diligently implemented by the Contractor.
 4. New Contractors must have an approved Plan before mobilizing workers to the site. Existing Contractors
    must have an approved Plan within six months of the date that World Bank financing is provided to the
    Project or as otherwise required by the contract. The Employer Representative and PMG E&S staff will be
    coordinating with the contractors on the implementation of the relevant construction activity emergency
    response procedures.Dam safety and impacts related thereto and management is included in the dam
    safety Emergency Preparedness Plan.

20. DEFINITIONS
 5. Emergency. An emergency is an unforeseen situation that presents an immediate risk to life, health,
    property, community, or the environment and that requires immediate action and/or an urgent need for
    help. An emergency is usually unexpected, urgent, and dangerous.
 6. Preparedness. The state of readiness to respond to emergency situations that may reasonably occur and
    that may affect the works, the workers, community or property. Readiness is achieved based on a
    continuous cycle of planning, organizing, training, equipping, exercising, evaluating, and taking corrective
    action or otherwise responding to incidents, emergencies and near misses. It involves risk assessment,
    development of prevention strategies, ensuring critical supplies such as medical equipment, personal
    protective equipment (PPE), and other necessities are available and maintained, and ensuring people have
    the knowledge, skills, and equipment necessary to take action.
 7. Response. The response to the actions taken in the immediate aftermath of an emergency. It consists of
    actions, with saving lives and alleviating suffering the most important, followed by reducing economic
    losses. The response phase comprises the coordination and management of resources, emergency rescue
    and medical care, firefighting, and sometimes search and rescue. Emergency response occurs in form of
    proactive/preemptive actions and or in the immediate aftermath of an emergency and typically involves
    first responders such as firefighters or first-aiders who are responsible for rescuing individuals from harm’s
    way or taking other action to mitigate further damage, and medical care for those affected by the
    emergency.

 Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                   Page | 60
23. TYPES OF EMERGENCIES
 11. The Baseline Risk Assessment completed as required by the Occupational Health and Safety Management
     Plan Framework must identify the emergency situations that may reasonably arise at the construction site
     or during activities performed by the Contractor. Planning and response strategy will depend on the type
     of emergency.
 12. Among the events and conditions that can escalate to become emergency situations, are the following:
    •   Earthquake. Rogun is in a moderately seismic zone and earthquakes could cause landslides or
        collapse of buildings or even tunnels. Clear evacuation plans are a necessity, as are communication
        networks inter-Project among managers and OHS personnel and cooperative agreements with
        local first responders.
    •   Fire. Fires can be catastrophic in a confined space such as a tunnel. Heat, smoke, and oxygen
        deprivation can injure or kill workers, and the heat can destroy equipment or weaken tunnel
        structures. Among other key elements of prevention and response are having appropriate
        firefighting equipment at hand, and trained personnel to use it.
    •   Accidents and injuries to workers: Accidents that injure that may require immediate intervention
        or other response action may include rock falls, falling from height, tripping and falling, eye
        injuries, burns, crushing, cuts, poisoning.
    •   Traffic accidents. These may involve pedestrians or operators/drivers and passengers. The Traffic
        and Transport Management Plan includes requirements to avoid or reduce such emergencies,
        including such things as proper road and site marking, reducing speed, traffic direction signs, and
        barriers.
    •   Collapse and Cave-In. Collapses of structures or ground during construction can cause death,
        injuries, or entrapment of workers. Measures to prevent or reduce the likelihood of emergencies
        include soil analysis, structural reinforcement, roof support, rock anchors, working in safe areas.
        Ensuring the availability of emergency rescue equipment is also essential.
    •   Equipment Failures. Malfunctions of construction machinery can lead to injuries or material
        damage. Implementing regular maintenance and inspection programs, training operators and
        workers on equipment usage, and having spare equipment are necessary measures.
    •   Explosives-related emergencies. Flying rock fragments or debris during the explosion can cause
        injuries to workers and can destroy or damage equipment, as well as causing cave-ins or rock
        fall/tunnel collapses. Additionally, high noise and shock waves can lead to hearing loss or other
        physical traumas. Availability of first aid equipment and trained personnel are essential.
    •   Electrocution: Accidental exposure to electricity can cause shocks or death if applicable
        requirements of the Occupational Health and Safety Plan are not properly implemented and if
        cables are cut accidentally during construction activities, and some incidents can also cause fires.
        Marking cables and isolators, and having trained personnel for rapid response during electrical
        outages are important.
    •   Fuel Leaks and Chemical Fires: Leakage of fuel from construction machinery or storage tanks can
        cause fires upon contact with sparks or heat. Some chemicals used in construction may be
        flammable, which increase the risk of fire. Availability of fire extinguishers, proper storage and



 Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
 Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                           October 2025
 Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                      Page | 61
       transport of flammable and hazardous materials, and providing fire safety training to workers are
       important.
   •   Extreme Temperatures. The Project experiences both extreme heat and extreme cold. Workers
       are at risk of heatstroke and dehydration, especially in summer months, and this can be avoided
       by providing regular water breaks, shaded areas, and training workers on heatstroke symptoms.
       Workers are also at risk of hypothermia and frostbite during winter. Risks can be reduced by
       providing warm break/recovery areas, providing windbreaks, proper and PPE.
   •   Injuries. Cuts, fractures, sprains, and other physical injuries can occur frequently. Availability of
       first aid supplies, ensuring workers use safety equipment, and providing occupational safety
       training are important.
   •   Heart Attacks and Strokes. Intense physical exertion during work can lead to serious health
       problems such as heart attacks or strokes, and this can be exacerbated by exposure to dust and
       other air pollutants. Regular health check-ups and availability of emergency response equipment
       are necessary, along with providing health and safety training to workers. Proper ventilation in
       tunnels and other dusty areas also can reduce medical issues at work.
   •   Drowning. Heavy rains can result in flooding of roads and work areas, and working near water can
       place workers and equipment at risk of falling in. Checking and reinforcing drainage systems and
       regularly monitoring weather forecasts are necessary precautions, as are placing flotation devices,
       signs, and barriers where there is danger of falling in.
   •   Landslides. There is a risk of landslides during work on slopes. Geologic surveys, surveys, applying
       stabilization techniques, constant observation and warning systems are necessary.
   •   Lack or Misuse of Protective Equipment. Injuries can occur or be more severe if workers do not use
       adequate personal protective equipment and use it properly. Providing and enforcing the proper
       use of PPE is an essential component of the safety program.
   •   Electrical and Mechanical Failures. Electrical installations or machinery can fail during incidents
       and also cause incidents. Either can cause electric shock or mechanical injury and also can serve
       to impede emergency response. Backup power sources for electrical outages and emergency
       procedures for mechanical failures have to be established.
   •   Winter Weather Conditions (Heavy Snow, Icing). Severe winter-weather conditions such as heavy
       snowfall and ice can render roads hazardous or impassable. Coordinated snow ploughing, salting,
       and real-time traffic updates are necessary to keep roads clear and safe.
   •   Sabotage and Terrorist Attacks. Acts of sabotage or terrorist attacks targeting highways or the
       Project itself can cause major disruptions and pose serious safety threats. Immediate security
       measures and coordination with law enforcement and the military are crucial for handling such
       incidents.
   •   Vehicle and Equipment Failures. Failure of construction or maintenance equipment can lead to
       hazardous situations and delays. Regular maintenance schedules and the availability of backup
       equipment are essential for continuous operation.
   •   Onset and outbreak of communicable diseases such as COVID-19.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                            October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                       Page | 62
24. TEAMS

 1.       EPRP TEAM (PLANNING TEAM)
 13. The EPRP will designate a team of subject matter experts from different various internal organizations to
     identify and prioritize potential emergencies and determine the overall scope of the plan, which will
     address each of the five phases of emergency management for each of the potential emergencies. Planning
     for some phases will be similar or identical for different emergencies, but they will need to be tailored
     based on the characteristics of the emergency and to some extent the probability of its occurrence.
      •   Prevention
      •   Mitigation
      •   Preparedness
      •   Response
      •   Recovery.
 14. For every identified potential emergency, the EPRP team must compile a procedure or procedures that
     addresses all five phases. Again, there will be similarities among the phases and among the emergencies,
     but each procedure will need to be tailored for the emergency and the phase.
 15. The EPRP team will need to coordinate its planning with the ER, other Contractors, community leaders,
     and may collaborate to form joint teams as appropriate to address emergencies that could affect multiple
     work crews and multiple Contractors.

 2.       EMERGENCY TEAMS
 16. The EPRP team must also designate individuals who will be assigned to a series of other teams to handle
     various aspects of planning and response and identify the training that each member of each team must
     receive. Training must emphasize the specific responsibilities of the trainee and the skills they will need.
 17. Key teams typically include at least the following:
      •   Firefighting
      •   Emergency rescue
      •   First-aid team
      •   Emergency co-ordinators
      •   Emergency wardens.
      •   Community Leaders/ER focal points
      •   Public Information Officer (that will manage communication to ensure accurate and timely
          information transmission).




 Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
 Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                            October 2025
 Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                       Page | 63
25. COMMUNICATIONS AND EARLY WARNING SYSTEMS
 18. Reliable communication is key to rapid response to emergencies. The EPRP will specify personnel lines and
     responsibilities among emergency team members and between/among emergency teams, and with
     management. The EPRP also must emergency protocols to outline how personnel within Contractor’s
     organization will be notified and instructed during and after an incident, and protocols for communications
     with other Contractors who may be involved in an emergency.
 19. The EPRP must require that an up-to-date contact list with names, responsibility, and contact numbers be
     displayed in all offices, workshops, meeting rooms, eating rooms, kitchens, stores, etc.
 20. The EPRP will include information on the best/most suitable communication channels and time for
     reaching and receiving feedback from communities
 21. Establish redundant communication channels (e.g., radio, telephone, SMS, sirens, social media) for
     emergency alerts.
 22. Develop clear and concise warning messages tailored to different audiences (public, response agencies,
     media).
 23. Conduct regular tests and drills of public warning systems.
 24. Maintain a 24/7 monitoring system capable of rapid response.
 25. The EPRP will require that only designated personnel communicate with external parties, and require that
     this be an element of training for all personnel.

26. ACTIVATION AND NOTIFICATION PROCEDURES
    •   Define activation criteria (e.g., detection of dam instability, unusual water flows, seismic events,

    •   Alert the State Service of Industrial Safety and Mining Operation (SSS) and, if needed, external
        responses agencies.
    •   Simultaneously alert the public in at-risk areas via all available channels.
    •   Keep detailed logs of notifications and actions taken.

27. RESOURCES FOR EMERGENCIES
 27. The EPRP will require that emergency response equipment (see bullet 6 in section 10) be recorded in an
     up-to-date register and inspected on a monthly basis to ensure its availability, correct quantity, and
     readiness for use. The EPRP will also include the requirements listed in the subsections below, and will
     provide the format that the various registers are to use.

 3.      FIREFIGHTING EQUIPMENT
 28. All firefighting equipment (extinguishers, sandboxes, water hoses, tools, etc.) is to be numbered
     individually and recorded in a register. A competent person must inspect every item monthly using a
     checklist and record the results.

 4.      MEDICAL EQUIPMENT
 29. All first-aid boxes and contents are to be recorded in a register, with a copy also kept in the box. Removal
     and use of any item from a box must be recorded on the register. A competent person must inspect
     monthly using a checklist, re-fill the box for all items used, record the results. If items are missing but not
     “signed out”, the inspector must report this, which will be investigated to determine the cause and to
     trigger a toolbox talk on the need to record use of first-aid supplies (that is, to point out that a missing item
     is not available for the next person who needs it).
 30. The EPRP itself is to describe the locations and capabilities of all clinics, hospitals, and other medical
     resources that are available, including ambulances, and provide contact information. The EPRP will all
     designate a member of the EPRP planning team to coordinate with all available such resource. This will
     involve informing them of the types of trauma and other medical emergencies that might occur and
     establishing clear lines of communication with the various emergency teams.

 5.      OTHER EQUIPMENT AND DEVICES
 31. Other equipment might include: safety equipment (for example smoke and heat detectors, fire blankets,
     emergency lighting and exit signs), Personal Protective Equipment (for example breathing equipment,
     chemical-resistant gloves and boots, eye protection and face shields), Hazardous Materials Response (for
     example spill containment kits, non-sparking tools, absorbent pads and booms, decontamination showers
     and stations, neutralizing agents), communication and coordination devices (for example two-way radios,
     satellite phones, public address systems, emergency notification systems, sirens, etc.), emergency
     response vehicles, documentation and mapping (for example emergency planning maps with evacuation
     routes, refuge areas, equipment locations; inventory checklists and maintenance logs; contracts with
     external response organizations).




 Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
 Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                                October 2025
 Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                           Page | 66
28. EVACUATION ROUTES AND MUSTER POINTS
 32. One or more evacuation routes must be available and identified for all places where workers may be on
     the construction site. Where possible, there should be at least two ways for workers to move away from
     any location where an emergency could occur.
 33. The EPRP will require that maps/diagrams of evacuation routes be prepared and placed in at least the
     following locations:
     •   Near the doorways of large rooms and caverns
     •   Near all stairs and elevators
     •   In all conference rooms
     •   In areas where emergency exits and evacuation routes are not readily apparent
     •   In well-lit spaces for employees to be able to see the map
     •   In the vicinity of designated emergency exits
     •   In high-traffic common areas
     •   At all junctions where tunnels meet (and visible in every direction)
     •   Accommodation and office hallways.
 34. Muster points are locations where workers not involved in an emergency response must report. There
     must be at least one muster point for every workplace and every other location where workers may be.
     The locations must be outside the range of effect of an emergency.
 35. Muster points are to be clearly marked and easily visible in ambient lighting.
 36. Community-based early warning and early response plan/actions including communication equipment,
     mobile phones, and toll free lines, sirens and local and culturally appropriate communication channels
     such as town criers etc.

30. TRAINING, DRILLS, AND PLAN MAINTENANCE

 6.
 38. Training is essential so that team members and workers are familiar with the types of emergencies that
     could occur, what could happen to them and to their colleagues, and what their responsibilities are (in
     most cases, the worker’s responsibility will be to move to a safe place and follow instructions):
      •   Conduct regular training sessions for all personnel and stakeholders involved in dam safety and
          emergency response.
      •   Organize annual evacuation drills involving the public and emergency services.
      •   Review and revise the EPRP at least once a year
      •   Keep all contact lists, maps, and resources inventory up to date.



 39. The EPRP will identify training requirements for all personnel, community leaders and members including
     the EPRP team itself, the emergency teams, and workers.

 7.       DRILLS AND EXERCISES
 40. The EPRP will require that there at least one drill per year be conducted for every potential emergency
     identified. It is noted that two or more potential emergencies can be combined in one exercise that shall
     include the active involvement and participation of riparian communities.
 41. The Planning Team will designate personnel to observe the performance of each team, and the non-
     involved workforce, during each drill and to record their findings so that preparatory activities, equipment,
     response actions, and other elements of the planning can be improved or corrected.
 42. The EPRP will require that there be a post-mortem meeting immediately following the drill to gather
     insights from emergency team members and leaders, observers, and others as to whether and how
     emergency planning and response should be changed (if necessary) to improve communication, responses
     times, response actions, and/or recovery.
      The results of the post-mortem meeting and the reports of the observers will be consolidated into an
      Emergency Drill Report that describes the drill and its outcome. Each such report will include
      recommendations for improvements in each element of the emergency response program. As needed,
      such improvements will result in revised training, modified responsibilities of individuals and teams,
      additional or different equipment, or any other element of the program..



 43. Public Education and Community Engagement: Community awareness, participation, and
     feedback, is an integral part of the EPRP. Some of the critical actions in this regard include:
 44. Distribute informational materials explaining risks, warning signals, and evacuation protocols.
 45. Engage community leaders and organizations in promoting preparedness and resilience.
 46. Host town hall meetings and open forums for questions and feedback.
 47. Encourage personal and family emergency planning among residents.

32. MANAGEMENT OF CHANGE
 50. The Contractor’s Management of Change procedure will be initiated when there is a material change to
     the EPRP. This would include new emergencies or other changes that require immediate modification of
     emergency response—changes in planning may await the next regular review.
 51. Trigger the formal Management-of-Change procedure ensures that all relevant authorities/persons are
     informed, all relevant management plans, procedures, and method statements are reviewed,
     revised/revised/updated and approved and all relevant persons are trained.
 52. The EPRP Plan will be reviewed and updated, in its entirety or in part, in the following instances:
      •   At a minimum and in its entirety, semi-annually (that is, twice per year)
      •   Following drills, as recommended by the Emergency Drill Report
      •   When major equipment goes out of service and when major new equipment becomes available
      •   Following all emergencies
 53. It is noted that some changes may occur without triggering an immediate revision to the EPRP. Such
     changes could include modifications to the contact list, changes in the Emergency Teams, changes to
     contact numbers, etc. These changes will be implemented immediately and changed in the EPRP in the
     next scheduled update.

    •   Pre-scripted public alert messages, media releases, and agency notifications.

 Annex v: Evacuation Plan/Evacuation Routes and Shelters
   • Identify and map primary and secondary evacuation routes.
   • Clearly signpost evacuation paths and distribute maps to the public.
   • Designate shelters in safe zones, equipped with essential supplies.


 Annex vi: Evacuation Procedures
   • Trigger evacuation orders promptly when risk thresholds are met.
   • Provide transportation for those without personal vehicles, including the elderly and individuals
       with disabilities.
   • Deploy law enforcement to direct traffic and assist with orderly evacuation.
   • Maintain family reunification processes at shelters.
   • Document and track evacuees for accountability.

34. COMMUNITY HEALTH AND SAFETY PLAN
 INTRODUCTION
 The Rogun Hydropower Project (HPP) located in the Republic of Tajikistan, is currently undergoing
 an extensive phase of construction and partial operation, with two turbines already in active use.
 The Project is owned by the Rogun Open Joint Stock Company (JSC) (hereafter referred to as
 ‘Rogun JSC’) and managed by the Project Management Group (PMG) for Energy Facilities
 Construction under the President of the Republic of Tajikistan. Rogun JSC is responsible for
 overseeing both the construction and long-term operation of the Project.
 This document is the Community Health and Safety Management Plan (CHSMP), developed as part
 of the Volume III, Environmental and Social Management Plan (ESMP) of the Environmental and
 Social Impact Assessment (ESIA).
 This plan is based on the findings of the studies within the ESIA, as well as the Labour Management
 Audit and Resettlement Audit.
 The purpose of this report is to establish the minimum criteria for community health and safety
 requirements and expectations that will be taken for the Project.
 The objectives of this CHSMP are to:
        Support Rogun PMG in meeting the requirements of the Project’s ESMP and for community
         health and safety management.
        Ensure compliance with all applicable standards, frameworks, and guidelines outlined in the
         World Bank Environmental and Social Standards (ESS) and relevant regulations of the
         Republic of Tajikistan.
        Provide and maintain a safe and healthy environment for Project-affected communities.
        Clarify roles and responsibilities within the Rogun HPP Project team, including contractors
         and subcontractors, and establish a framework for Rogun JSC to set and enforce
         community health and safety standards.
        Provide a foundation for continuous review and adaptation of community health and safety
         measures to reflect changing site conditions, construction progress, and operational phases.
 This CHSMP shall remain a living document, updated as necessary to maintain alignment with the
 Project objectives and applicable standards.
 SUPPORTING PLANS
 The following plans and documents support the CHSMP and should be read in conjunction with this
 document to ensure a coordinated approach to community health and safety:
        Volume 3: A1 Environmental and Social Commitments Register;
        Volume 3: A7 Security Management Plan;
        Volume 3: A9 Traffic Management Plan; and
        Volume 3: B12 Emergency Preparedness and Response Plan.

EXISTING CONDITIONS
Site Location
The Rogun Hydropower Project (HPP) is located in the Rogun region of Tajikistan, approximately
100 kilometres east of Dushanbe along the Vakhsh River. The surrounding area is characterized by
mountainous terrain and steep valleys typical of the region’s rugged topography.
The land surrounding the Project site is sparsely populated, with limited agricultural activities in
nearby villages and primarily undeveloped land. The Vakhsh River valley hosts multiple hydropower



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 89
facilities, with Rogun HPP positioned as the uppermost plant in this cascade, upstream of the Nurek,
Baipaza, Sangtuda 1, Sangtuda 2, and Golovnaya HPPs.
Further detail on the site location and project background is provided within Volume 1, Chapter 1:
Introduction and Volume 1, Chapter 3: Project Description of this ESIA.
Identified Risks
Community health and safety impacts will mainly affect the residential areas that are in close
proximity to the construction compounds for the Project, and roads used to access these
compounds. These residential areas, specifically Rogun City and Pasi Muhraho, will be highly
sensitive to potential health and safety impacts during construction.
For the construction phase, the following risks were identified related to community health and
safety;
       Potential noise pollution associated with construction work activities i.e. blasting, truck
        movements, excavations, ground works.
       Potential air pollution, mainly dust resulting from groundwork and excavation, but also from
        vehicle movements.
       Reduced safety of local families, women and children resulting from potential labour influx
        and movement of overseas workers into the area and potential local conflict, which could be
        exacerbated by security personnel.
       Reduced safety as a result of unauthorized access to construction works and equipment,
        obstruction of local access and road diversions, increased road traffic, including construction
        vehicles, and increased risk of road accidents.
       Potential exposure to hazardous materials and substances, such as asbestos, from
        demolition of houses in flooding-zone settlements and old factories, buildings (e.g., old
        cement factory to be dismantled to open up space for the incoming contractors to establish
        worker camps).
The construction activities for the Project include excavation and ground works which, in addition to
the risks from increase traffic, could lead to direct health and safety impacts within local
communities.
Outcomes from Focus Group Discussions (FGD) and Household Surveys suggest there are poor
road conditions that put community safety at risk, especially where high proportions of young
children live. Households with caring responsibilities, people with disabilities, and elderly people may
face restrictions on accessing some of their local community assets and infrastructure when in close
proximity to construction activities.
This CHSMP encompasses the actions required to mitigate effects on community health, safety and
security identified in the ESIA. Additional effects on community health and safety have been
identified through the completion of a Gender Action Plan, Resettlement Audit, Labour Management
Procedures, and through ongoing stakeholder engagement. Actions from these studies are also
included in this CHSMP to address these additional effects.
In addition to the risks identified in the ESIA, and associated studies, ESS4 emphasizes the need for
the Borrower to also address the following risks to local communities;
       Universal access – the concept of universal access should be applied in the design and
        construction of new buildings and structures (e.g. schools, public facilities, or roads).


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 90
       Emergency preparedness – measures need to be implemented to prevent any emergency
        event (including fire, explosion, leak or spill, as well as dam failure) from affecting the health
        and safety of communities.
       Ecosystem services – consideration of impacts on services derived from the ecosystem by
        affected communities need to be considered, including food and freshwater.
       Exposure to waterborne, water-based, water related and vector-borne diseases, and
        communicable and non-communicable diseases.
       Dam safety – measures need to be adopted and implemented throughout the lifecycle of the
        dam.
ROLES AND RESPONSIBILITIES
The effective implementation of this CHSMP relies on a site-wide understanding of roles and
responsibilities across all Project activities and personnel.
Rogun JSC, as the overseeing entity, holds ultimate responsibility for CHSMP enforcement and
alignment with the Project’s ESMP and other Applicable Standards. The Project Management
Consultant (PMC), acting as the Employer's Representative, will support by conducting regular
audits, approving contractor-specific CHSMPs, and reinforcing site-wide compliance with community
health and safety protocols.
The organizational structure for the Rogun HPP Project is illustrated in Figure 4-1. This structure
reflects the distribution of authority, oversight, and operational responsibilities among the Project
Management Group (PMG), Rogun JSC, the Project Management Consultant (PMC), and other key
stakeholders.
Rogun JSC holds ultimate responsibility for implementing the requirements of this CHSMP across all
Project activities, supported by the Project Management Consultant (PMC). The PMC shall provide
regular compliance checks, approve Lot-specific CHSMPs, and ensure site-wide adherence to each
minimum requirements and best practices. Contractors are required to align their operations with the
guidelines outlined in this CHSMP.

MONITORING
Table 5-1 includes a summary of the monitoring requirements to be delivered under the CHSMP,
together with the pathways to inform adaptive management on the Project. This table should be
updated as required (e.g. upon findings from additional survey effort) to ensure an effective
monitoring strategy is adopted.
Monitoring of community health and safety should be undertaken to:
Assess the effectiveness of mitigation actions, and other related actions/controls;
Assess actual impacts against predicted impacts; and
Assess compliance with applicable legal and other requirements.
Mitigation measures identified in Table 5-1 should be in place no later than 60 days after the Project
becomes effective, remain for the duration of the Project, and as per any relevant associated plans
including the SEP, RLRF, RAP 2/LRP 2 and LMP.
Monitoring of the KPIs set out in Table 5-1 will be the responsibility of the parties identified in Table
5-1. Performance against the KPIs will be reported on a monthly basis.
Actions arising from monitoring of the KPIs will be collated into the centralized Corrective Action
Register (CAR) as detailed in the ESMP and reviewed monthly.
REPORTING
Any community health or safety incidents which meet the requirements for incident notification must
be reported with the incident management procedures as described in the ESMP.
Monthly internal reports will be generated by the E&S Manager to report any community health or
safety related incidents that have occurred across the Project.
PMG will review all community health and safety reports to ensure actions and feedback required
are completed and recorded.

Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 104
35. OCCUPATIONAL HEALTH AND SAFETY
 PURPOSE AND APPLICABILITY
 Each Contractor employed at Rogun Hydropower Project (the Project) will prepare and implement
 an Occupational Health and Safety Management Plan (OHS Plan, or Plan) that is consistent with
 this Framework. The Plan will be reviewed and approved by the Contractor’s Project Manager and
 submitted to the Employer’s Representative (ER) and/or the Project Management Consultant
 (ER/PMC) for review. Once approved, it shall be diligently implemented by the Contractor.
 New Contractors must have an approved Plan before mobilizing workers to the site. Existing
 Contractors must have an approved Plan within six months of the date that World Bank financing is
 provided to the Project or as otherwise required by the contract. If an existing Contractor has an
 OHS Plan that has been approved by the ER, a new plan is not needed if the ER verifies the
 existing plan is equivalent to what is required by this Framework, or if the Contractor makes
 sufficient changes to the existing plan that the ER determines it is equivalent.
 The purpose of this Framework is to identify the minimum requirements for the Contractors’ OHS
 Plans, and to ensure the Plans contains the required content, although the Plan may vary based on
 the potential hazards and risks of the works the Contractor is required to perform.
 APPLICABLE STANDARDS
 The Plan must include such requirements are necessary to ensure the Contractors’ works will meet
 the requirements of Tajikistan law, standards promulgated by International Financial Institutions, the
 international obligations of Tajikistan, and good international industry practice (GIIP), Environmental
 and Social Standard 2 (Labor and Working Conditions) as summarized below. In all cases, site-
 specific engineering and scientific judgment will be used to confirm that standards are sufficient to
 prevent unacceptable impacts on the people and environment. If there are inconsistencies among or
 between requirements of Tajikistan law and international standards, the Plan must adopt the more
 stringent unless a standard conflicts with national law, in which case national law applies.
 Tajikistan Legislation
 The Labor Code of the Republic of Tajikistan (No. 1329, 23 July 2016) governs all aspects of
 employment, including occupational health and safety of workers. Articles 349 and 352 of the law
 specifically include construction and operation of facilities in the types of occupations that are
 subject to the Code and required to provide for the safety of workers at all workplaces (§331).
 Other key requirements for employers include:
        Provide safe working conditions and be responsible for noncompliance (§331)
        Provide information on risks to health and safety and provide information on working
         conditions annually (§332)
        Ensure pre-employment and regular medical examinations, and on-demand examinations,
         of workers with hazardous working conditions, including underground and night work (§333)
        Provide personal protection equipment and tools at no cost to the employee (§335)
        Instruct employees on safety rules prior to allowing them to work (§336)




 Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
 Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                    October 2025
 Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                              Page | 105
       Provide a new “easier job”, and re-train the employee, if a doctor determines that an
        employee cannot fulfil their duties due to disability, occupational disease, or other job-
        related “disorder” (§337)
       Provide workers with sanitation and amenities, including drinking water, showers, rest areas,
        and other amenities, as well as medical facilities (§340)
       Provide social insurance for accidents and diseases (§341)
       Investigate the causes of job-related accidents and issue a timely investigation report, which
        must be provided to the worker, who may appeal the findings to an employee representative
        or the courts (§342)
       Pay “damage compensation” to an employee who suffers a job-related disability and pay the
        family of a worker who dies in a job-elated accident an allowance of at least five years’
        salary, exclusive of “damage compensation”, which must still be paid to the employee’s
        beneficiaries and children (§343).
Key obligations for workers include the following (§346):
       Pass medical examinations
       Accept and use PPE and other tools required for protection
       Attend safety training
       Following medical instructions if resulting from employer-funded examinations
       Inform line supervisor of accidents, disease-causing factors, and other threats to life and
        health
Workers may leave their job if they believe their lives are in danger and they are not provided with
the tools to eliminate the danger. In such cases, they must inform their supervisor or safety unit
(§337)
Not only state officials have the right to inspect premises to verify safety conditions, but trade unions
and other employee representatives also may conduct inspections of working conditions, participate
in accident investigations, and propose measures to eliminate violations, which must be considered
by the employer (§357).
International Conventions and Protocols
The International Labor Organization considers that two of its “core” conventions are related to
occupational health and safety:
       Occupational Safety and Health Convention, 1981 (No. 155) requires countries to adopt
        policies on OHS and the working environment with the intention to prevent accidents and
        injuries accidents at work. Tajikistan ratified this convention in 2009.
       Promotional Framework for Occupational Safety and Health Convention, 2006 (No. 187).
        This convention requires countries to promote its OHS programs. Tajikistan has not ratified
        this convention.
International Standards
The various international financial institutions that will provide financing for construction and
operation of Rogun HPP also have requirements related to OHS that their Borrowers are required to
meet. Each of these institutions require that projects comply with not only national legislation but
also the institution’s own standards and Good International Industry Practice (GIIP). In general, the



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment

standards of international financial institutions have the same intentions and goals as national
legislation.
OHS standards of the international financial institutions are exemplified by World Bank
Environmental Social Standard 2, “Labor and Working Conditions”. Among the key requirements are
the following:
       Identification of risks and hazards (¶25).
       Provision of preventive and protective measures
       Training of workers and maintenance of training record
       Documenting and reporting occupational accidents, diseases and incidents
       Emergency prevention, preparedness, and response
       Access to canteens, hygiene facilities, rest areas, properly managed accommodation
       Provision of personal protective equipment without expense to the project workers
       Provide means for workers to report unsafe or unhealth work environments, allow them to
        remove themselves from such environments, and do not require them to return to work until
        corrective actions are taken (¶27)
       Regular review of OHS performance and the working environment and taking action to
        address issues (¶30)
       Collaboration among employers in applying OSH requirements when their employees are
        required to work together (29)
       Inclusion by Contractors of equivalent requirements and noncompliance remedies in
        subcontracts (32).
The most widely recognized GIIP are provided by a series of Environmental, Health, and Safety
(EHS) Guidelines that are promulgated by the World Bank Group. The EHS Guidelines are technical
reference documents that provide examples of GIIP for occupational health and safety and
environmental protection. EHS Guidelines that are relevant to the various activities involved in
Rogun HPP construction and/or operation include the General EHS Guidelines, which provides OHS
guidance for construction and operation of facilities in all industry sectors, and a series of sector-
specific EHS Guidelines. Many of the sector-specific EHS Guidelines provide OHS-related guidance
for activities that occur at the Rogun and HPP /or DFZ construction sites, including:
       Cement and Lime Manufacturing (2022): cement, grout handling/batching
       Construction Materials Extraction (2007): quarries, material handling
       Crude Oil and Petroleum Product Terminals (2007): diesel storage and transport
       Electric Power Transmission and Distribution: substations and transmission lines (2007)
       Health Care Facilities (2007): clinics
       Mining (2007): underground works, material handling
       Retail Petroleum Networks (2007): shops, vehicle repair, etc.
       Waste Management Facilities (2007): nonhazardous and hazardous waste management
       Water and Sanitation (2007): water supplies and storage
