MANAGEMENT APPROACH AND ACTIVITIES
Each Contractor’s OHS Plan must describe what tasks are required to be performed by all workers,
when and where the tasks must be undertaken, what hazards and risks may be faced by the worker,
and what must be done to eliminate or reduce the hazards and to avoid or reduce the risks.
Therefore, Plan must include all the activities within the contractor’s control or influence that can
impact the contractor’s health and safety performance. This is the basis of the Hazard Identification
and Risk Assessment, as described below.
In order to develop an effective OHS Plan, the Contractor must
       Understand the external and internal issues that are relevant to the Project’s purpose and
        that may affect the Project’s ability to meeting applicable standards and protect its workers
       Identify and understand the needs and expectations of workers and other interested parties
        who may affect the safety and health of the workers or the management of OHS hazards
        and risks
       Understand and describe ongoing and/or planned work-related activities, on and off the
        construction site, that will be undertaken by the Contractor or that need to be managed in
        order to protect the workers. Activities must be those for this project, not generic activities
        that are often covered in corporate-level plans.
       Taking all of the above into consideration, every Contractor shall conduct a Baseline Hazard
        Identification and Risk Assessment (HIRA) to identify all hazards and risks and how such
        hazards and risks will be mitigated and/or managed to an acceptable level. The Baseline
        HIRA shall be provided and submitted to the Employer’s Representative (ER) and/or the
        Project Management Consultant (ER/PMC) for review. (See paragraph 22 below)
CONTENT OF OHS PLAN
Each Contractor’s OHS Plan will cover the topics described in this section and either include or
reference specific procedures, method statements, and health and safety documents that provide
additional details on tasks and performing work safely.
The OHS Plan must describe how Corporate and Project management will demonstrate leadership
and commitment to the health and safety of workers. The Plan must identify specific measures that
will be undertaken, which could include such things as ensuring the OHS Policy is established and
that other corporate objectives are compatible with the Policy, providing resources to establish,
implement, maintain and improve the OH&S management system are available, conforming to the
requirements of the OHS Plan and other relevant plans, protecting workers from reprisals if they
report incidents, hazards, risks and opportunities, and providing process(es) for consultation and
participation of workers.
Provide the corporate and/or Project Occupational Health & Safety Policy in the language(s) spoken
by workers and signed by the Chief Executive Officer(or equivalent top manager) and/or Project
Manager, and a short description of where and how it will be displayed and communicated. The
Policy must include commitments to provide a safe and healthy working conditions for the
prevention of work-related injury and illness, fulfill legal requirements, and to ensure workers are
aware of the risks to which they may be exposed and the measures, that are required to avoid or
reduce the risks to acceptable levels, including their own responsibilities.
Define the organizational roles and responsibilities, and their responsibilities and authorities, of all
managers, workplace/team supervisors and personnel responsible for worker safety. This will

include Subcontractor roles and responsibilities under the Contractor’s OHS Plan and/or under an

requirements of this Framework. The Plan must also describe in detail how language barriers
between those who supervise work and OHS and the workers for whom they are responsible.
Describe how health and safety representatives will be elected, appointed, trained, and conduct
inspections. A health and safety representative is a person elected by his co-workers to present
them on health and safety matters, conduct monthly health and safety inspections during normal
working hours, assist with incident/accident investigations, and attend safety meetings conveying
the OHS issues reported by his/her co-workers and/or which were identified during inspection. A
manager/supervisor may not be elected as a health and safety representative.
Describe the form in which health and safety information will be conveyed to workers (induction,
safety briefings, toolbox talks, etc.), when and how often they will be held (daily, weekly, etc.), who
will attend, who will lead, who will keep minutes, and how the minutes will be distributed (text, email,
posterboards, etc. Also, identify (by position) the members of a health and safety committee, with
written duties and functions, and how often they will meet with Project management to convey OHS
issues reported to them or identified during safety inspections. Elected health and safety
representatives must be members of the health and safety committee.
Complete a Hazard Identification and Risk Assessment Procedure based on and equivalent to that
presented in Appendix 1 (Hazard Identification and Risk Assessment (HIRA) Procedure). Identify
who will conduct both Baseline and Detailed Risk Assessments and indicate when the assessments
will be completed and how they will be conducted (that is, on what it will be based). The “Baseline
Risk Assessment” is a high-level assessment, while the Detailed Risk Assessment will identify and
control/mitigate the risks of every job/task that will be performed. The Detailed Risk Assessment will
identify/define the controls and mitigation measures that are required in order to avoid or reduce the
risks to acceptable levels. Employees conducting Hazard Identification and Risk Assessments
(HIRAs) must be trained in the methodology on how to conduct a HIRA. Appendix 1 includes
provides an example of a risk assessment.
The OHS Plan will provide details on how the mitigation measures and control identified in the Risk
assessment will be implemented. This will include details of:
       Actions that must be taken to avoid, control, reduce, or otherwise mitigate risks
       Who be responsible for taking the actions, including the qualifications and training that will
        be required for each position and for actions that require performance by persons
        designated as competent persons. A “competent person” is a person who has the required
        knowledge, training, skills, experience to be responsible for performing a specific task and,
        where applicable, the qualifications. Among occupations that must be performed by a
        competent person would be blasters, welders, drivers, operators and those who work at
        height, in confined spaces. Competent in the sense of knowledge, experience, skill and
        where applicable, qualifications. Because every worker who will undertake tasks will have
        certain responsibilities to avoid/manage risks, this include not only OHS specialists but all
        other workers as well, including day laborers, drivers, banksmen, cleaners, welders, etc. etc.
       Program for training workers of the risks they may fact and the mitigations/controls that they
        and others must take in order to avoid or reduce the risks.
       Resources that will be required and provided (e.g., office space, computers, communication
        devices, vehicles, instruments, equipment, personal protective equipment, etc.)

       How the implementation of the actions will be verified through inspections and other means

REQUIREMENTS
This section provides an outline of minimum requirements that must be specified in the OHS plan.
Based on the detailed Hazard Identification and Risk Assessment, the OHS Plan must describe the
measures that will be taken to protect workers from injury and illness. This will involve the
development and listing of Procedures, Method Statements, and various Documents and Forms.
Indicative safety-related Procedures are listed in Appendix 2, indicative Method Statements are
listed in Appendix 3, and other relevant Environmental and Social Management Plans are listed in
section 9 below. When details are provided in one of these documents, the OHS Plan will
summarize key requirements and incorporate by reference the requirements of the Procedure,
Method Statement, Document, or Management Plan.
The OHS Plan must provide details of how machinery and equipment will be made safe for
operation, including measures for mechanical, electrical installations, personal, and general
safeguarding. When requirements are recorded in Method Statements, these may be incorporated
by reference and summarized in the OHS Plan. Similarly, when the requirements are recorded in
various Environmental and Social Management Plans (see section 9 below), these Plans may be
referenced and summarized.
Mechanical Safeguarding requirements must be identified and implemented based on a complete
inventory of machinery and plant, lifting machines and tackle, mobile plant and machinery, machine
guards, formwork and falsework, that will be used in performing the works. The OHS Plan and/or
associated Procedures must identify what mechanical aids will be used for what task and how it will
be used by trained/competent/authorized operators, inspected, and serviced so as to maintain safe
operation. In addition, the Plan must describe how rotating shafts, grinders, conveyors, and other
such machinery will be guarded to protect workers from injury and death. Finally, the Plan must
describe who will design formwork and falsework, including scaffolding, the standard/legislative
requirements that will be met, and what records will be kept.
To safeguard Electrical Installations, the Plan must describe who will install them (that is, the
“competent person” who will be authorized to do so), inspect, and verified as safe, and how
temporary installations will be safeguarded in an equivalent manner. For Portable Electrical
Equipment and Tools, the Plan must describe how they will be used, who may use them, who will
inspect the Portable Electrical Equipment and Tools, and what records will be kept.
Many other activities will also require Safeguarding that must be described in the Plan, including:
       Excavations and trenches (approximate depth, length, locations), including how workers will
        be protected against accidental falling into excavations, who and how excavations will be
        inspected, and how safe access / egress into or out from excavations will be provided.

       Water Environs. Describe how for workers who may work near water or otherwise be in

Personal Safeguarding. The Plan must describe measures that are not associated with specific
workplaces and activities, including the following:
       Personal Protective Equipment. Describe the minimum PPE required of all persons on site
        and the specialized PPE that will be required for certain locations and tasks. Specialized
        PPE may be described in detail in various Procedures and/or Method Statements and
        summarized in the OHS Plan. The OHS Plan must describe the selection, provision,
        replacement, training and maintenance of PPE, etc. , and state clearly that PPE will be
        provided at no cost to the worker, and that no worker may perform work without required
        PPE. The OHS Plan also must specify how often PPE will be inspected (as opposed to daily
        checks by supervisors) and who will inspect it.

        summarize key requirements, including the means by which employees will be transported
        to and from the workplace to their quarters (including on-site accommodations), vehicle
        standards (e.g., mandatory safety belt usage), prohibitions on riding on unauthorized
        vehicles/equipment, and prohibitions on driver use of mobile phones.
       Worker Accommodations . The Accommodations Management Plan will include specific
        requirements for accommodations. The term “accommodations” includes not only workers’
        living quarters but also kitchens, sanitary and ablution facilities, rest areas, and all other
        areas that are provided by the employer where their work is not performed. It is noted that
        the Hazard Identification and Risk Assessment must cover the tasks performed by the
        workers who operate and manage worker accommodations.

        provide medical care, the number and locations of ambulances and other vehicles that carry
        patients, the locations and contents of first aid kits/boxes, the training of first aiders, and the
        ratio of first aiders to workers.
       Noise. The Noise Management Plan will include requirements to meet applicable standards.
        The OHS Plan should include the Noise Management Plan by reference and include a
        summary of key provisions

REPORTING
The OHS Plan will identify the means by which, and the level within the Contractor’s organization to
which reports will be made, and of what will be reported. At a minimum, the following formal reports
must be prepared. Except as noted, they will be reported in the monthly progress report required by
the contract.
       Violations of requirements that result in near significant near misses, on at least a monthly
        basis and near misses that occurred in spite of compliance
       First aid injuries and minor medical treatment
       Lost-time injuries, illnesses, or fatalities related to work or occurring on the construction site
        reported to Contractor management immediately (no later than the end of a work shift) and
        to the ER and/or Employer (as required by contract) within 24 hours.
       Number of inspections, safety and medical personnel available, open positions, number of
        violations, new safety equipment, safety equipment currently out of service, number and
        nature of violations, number of vehicles dedicated to medical and safety personnel, and
        other metrics required by the contract.
       OHS training record.
ROOT CAUSE ANALYSIS
The OHS Plan must include an Accident/Incident Investigation Procedure that describes who and
what will be investigated to determine the root cause(s) and contributing cause(s); a Root Cause


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                        October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                  Page | 113
Analysis is the process of examining what happened in order to establish how and why an adverse
event occurred. Two examples of forms that can be used for Root Cause Analysis are provided in
Appendix 5.
The Contractor will lead or participate (depending upon requirements of the Employer and/or the
Employer’s Representative) in a root cause analysis of all serious near misses, major property
damage, medical care and lost-time injuries, disabling injuries, and fatalities (see Appendix 6. The
root cause must identify preventive and/or corrective measures to prevent or minimize the risk of
recurrence of the adverse event.
Unless prohibited by law, the Contractor’s Root Cause Analysis will be conducted separately from,
and concurrently with, any investigation by the Service for State Supervision of Works Safety in
Industry and Mining
All preventive/corrective measures must follow a hierarchy of controls, in the order listed:
       Elimination
       Substitution
       Engineering controls
       Administrative (signage, warnings, etc.), and lastly
       Personal Protective Equipment (PPE).
All employees who may be required to conduct investigations must be trained in accident
investigations and root cause analysis. For investigations and root cause analyses led by the
Contractor, the Contractor must invite the Employer’s Representative to participate and to allow JSC
Rogun to participate if that is requested. In addition, the analysis will be carried out a Contractor
team that includes one or more of the following: senior engineer, OHS manager and/or specialist,
supervisor, and member of the safety committee (see paragraph 21).
All injury investigation must be submitted to the Employer’s Representative and/or the Project
Management Consultant for review and comment.
TRAINING
The OHS Plan to identify the training that will be provided to all staff and managers who will perform
work for the Contractor so they are able to perform their responsibilities at minimum risk to
themselves, other workers, the Project, and the environment. It must identify who will be responsible
for conducting the training and who will be trained in what skills. Training will focus on the assigned
responsibilities of the trainees, and will cover technical and administrative skills needed to perform
assigned responsibilities related to materials and waste management, the actions required in case
of incidents, and topics related to occupational health and safety, including personal protective
equipment required to conduct assigned activities.
Training must be sufficient so that workers know the risks to which they will be exposed and the
measures that have been and must be taken to avoid or minimize risks identified in the detailed
Hazard Identification and Risk Assessment. This will include training on general hazards and risks
as well as specific risks of their tasks, workplaces, and working conditions general safety training,
Only workers who have received the relevant training will be allowed to work with or be exposed to
hazardous materials.

MANAGEMENT OF CHANGE
The OHS Plan will be reviewed and updated, in its entirety or in part, in the following instances:
       At a minimum and in its entirety, semi-annually (that is, twice per year)
       When new tasks are to be performed
       When tasks are to be performed in new locations or with new equipment
       When Procedures and/or Method Statements are modified in such a way that previous
        requirements related to safety or health are no longer sufficient to protect workers
       Following accidents that resulted in fatality, disability, lost-time injury, medical treatment, and
        repeated first aid treatments or near misses
The OHS Plan must require that a Management of Change checklist be completed if there are
material changes to any Method Statement, Procedure, or the OHS Plan, in order to ensure all
relevant authorities/persons are informed, all relevant management plans, procedures, and method
statements are reviewed, revised/revised/updated and approved and all relevant persons are
trained. An indicative Management of Change form is provided as Appendix 7.

APPENDIX 1: EXAMPLE METHODOLOGY FOR HIRA
Example Methodology for Hazard identification and Risk Assessment
Introduction
An effective risk management process is an integral part of an efficient Occupational Health and
Safety Management System. This Appendix describes the steps to be taken for a Hazard
Identification and Risk Assessment (HIRA) to ensure so far as is reasonably practicable that health
and safety hazards are identified and controls implemented to eliminate or mitigate the risk(s) to
persons. This methodology can also be applied to assess community risks.
Purpose and Scope
The undertaking of risk assessments and documentation of the process will assist managers in
understanding the decisions made around health and safety. The Company hereby demonstrates
that it has considered and identified foreseeable OHS risks and taken action where practicable.
General
Hazard Identification and Risk Assessment (HIRA) is the process of identifying hazards that have
the potential to harm people (in the objects being used, work environment and / or the work
processes undertaken), and the process of evaluating the risk arising from a hazard(s), taking into
account the adequacy of existing controls, and deciding whether or not the risk(s) is acceptable.
Some risks are visible; however, others are only evident and able to be understood when a work
task is observed.
Obtain Information
A HIRA will be conducted by a team of employees and competent persons who have been trained
how to conduct a Risk Assessment. The team must include as a minimum an OHS Specialist, a
supervisor and at least one employee conducting the task/work.
Before commencing with a hazard identification and risk assessment, the Workplace Manager
and/or Risk Assessor must use as many as possible of the avenues below to obtain as much
information as possible with regards to hazards in their workplace, or on a specific task / activity,
namely: -
Workplace Manager and/or Risk Assessor to:
         Decide on the workplace routine activities that are to be performed on a regular basis to
          have a completed issue base risk assessment;
         Ensure that non-routine activities in the work place are considered;
         Human behaviour is considered when any risk assessment is being performed;
         Consult with the health & safety representative (HSR) and / or personnel in relation to the
          activities and tasks they perform;
         Undertake a walk-through of the workplaces;
         Examine previous similar work hazard, incident and injury reports;
         Refer to OHS hazard identification checklists contained within Company's various OHS
          procedures e.g. Manual handling procedure - manual handling hazard identification
          checklist, etc.;



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 116
       Refer to manufacturer's product information for plant / equipment;
       Review (if available) records of worksite inspections;
       Refer to legislative requirements and / or work safe guidance material;
       Consult with peers to clarify industry knowledge;
       Consult with HSE/OHS Officers as to findings of HSE/OHS audits / HSE/OHS reports; and
       Review minutes of health & safety committees.
Note: The identification of health and safety hazards to include special, ad hoc or extraordinary
events e.g. emergency situations.
Types of Risk Assessments

There are two main types of risk assessments namely:
       Baseline Risk Assessment: Performed before any task or project starts and is an overview
        (bird’s-eye view) on a specific project or site.
       A particular activity, task or area called an Issue Based (or Detailed) Risk Assessment:
        Carried out before any specific activity or task can be performed. If a manager has a number
        of risk assessments to complete, he/she must prioritise risk assessments based on the
        perceived risk to employees.
Managers must also identify all people that may be affected by the workplace hazards such as:
       Employees directly involved;
       'Neighbours' such as other workers, or a company’s operating at an adjacent site;
       Contractors, maintenance and cleaning employees; and
       Visitors or members of the public.
       Hazards originating outside the workplace capable of adversely affecting the health and
        safety of persons under the control of or within the workplace.
The Mini risk assessment and is conducted daily per task and per area by the Foreman / Supervisor
WITH the work team prior to commencement of the days’ work. The daily Mini RA should only cover
the portion of the activities carried out on the specific day and should be updated as changes occur.
The purpose of the Mini RA is to continually monitor and review hazards, risks & controls and to also
provide an opportunity for managing immediate changes in the physical work environment or work
scope that may affect the Health & Safety of persons as well as the surrounding environment.
Step 1 – Enter Workplace Detail
     Insert the workplace and task to be assessed in the first section of the Hazard Identification
         and Risk Assessment form
       Utilize as many persons as possible when performing a HIRA. Involve persons that have
        performed such activity and / or task previously. They normally have the most experience
        and knowledge about hazards and risks associated with the task and / or activity
Step 2 – Hazard Identification & Risk Assessment
     List all activities, tasks, equipment, plant and areas e.g. stores, crushers, etc.
       List all hazards and potential hazards
       List all risks and potential risks (Note that a hazard may have more than one risk.



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                               Page | 117
       List all the current controls that are in place. (Do not list controls that should be in place, or
        that you think will reduce the risk or that you would like to have in place).
       Evaluate every risk using the risk matrix. The use of a risk matrix assists one to better
        understand the severity of risk, which in turn enables managers to prioritise which hazards
        to address first. The risk matrix requires managers to consider:-
        o   The likelihood of the harm occurring. The likelihood considers the chance that the hazard
            will result in an injury / illness.
        o   The consequence of the potential harm. The consequence considers the potential harm
            to persons. If the hazard is not controlled how serious an injury is likely to occur?
            Managers must consider what controls are already in place to eliminate or minimise this
            outcome. When the worst possible outcome has been identified managers can circle the
            relevant consequence in the risk matrix.
       Select the most appropriate in all of the two above and insert the number in the relevant
        column. L = Likelihood and C= Consequence.
       Cross-reference the Likelihood and Consequence to obtain the risk rating (indicated as RR).
        The higher the risk rating assigned, the higher the level of risk associated with the hazard.
       Compare the score to the Risk Score Calculator below the matrix. Any risk with a resultant
        score of 13+ (High or Extreme) requires more controls or pro-active management. If more
        controls can be implemented, the risk to be re-assessed once the recommended control are
        completely implemented.
Step 3 – Control Selection / Recommendation / MP = Management Plan
     List all proposed, actual controls and / or recommendations based on the hierarchy of
         controls. Always start from the top and work your way down.
       Managers need to make sure that the risks are reduced to:
        o   A level that can be tolerated by the organisation having regard to its legal obligations and
            Policies
        o   As far as is 'reasonably practicable'.
       When identifying controls managers need to remember:-
        o   The more effective the control measures, the more lasting the effect of control;
        o   At times it may be necessary to implement more than one type of control to effectively
            manage the risks
        o   Sometimes a control can't be actioned straight away due to costs / resources;
        o   There may be a need to implement immediate (short term) and medium (long term)
            controls until such time a long term solution is implemented; and
        o   Controls should be selected in response to the level of risk posed by the hazards, and
            the practicability of the control.
       The hierarchy of controls is a useful tool which managers should consider when identifying
        controls. The control options at the top of the hierarchy are most effective; they do not
        require further management once they are implemented. The further down the hierarchy of
        controls, the more on-going management and effort is required in maintaining control over
        the hazard.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                        October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                  Page | 118
                                                             Safe place controls
                Most Effective                               Involves modifying the
                                  Elimination                workplace in some way
                                  Substitution               to eliminate or reduce
                                                             the risk
                                  Engineering
                                                             Safe person controls
                                  Administration
                                                             Requires the people in
                                  PPE                        the workplace to behave
                Least Effective                              in a particular way to
                                                             reduce the risk


       Elimination is the first control option that needs to be considered. If it is possible to cease
        the activity, no further risk control actions will be required in the future because the hazard
        no longer exists.
        o   Consideration: Is it possible to discontinue the use of a product / chemical / process /
            machine?
        o   Example: Abandonment of a practice which involves hazardous substances, etc.
       Substitution is where the item that poses a hazard is replaced by a similar item that does
        the same job, but has a lower hazard level.
        o   Consideration: Is there another chemical, machine, process that we can use that does
            the same job, but has lower levels of risk?
        o   Example: water based instead of solvent-based paint, etc.
       Engineering is changing the process, equipment or tools in such a way that the risk is
        reduced.
        o   Consideration: Is there any way to modify or change the machine / process / object so
            that a person is not directly exposed to the hazard? Is there a way to modify the object /
            process / machine so that the exposure is minimised? Is there any way in which the
            machine / object / process can be modified to give greater control over the process and
            potential exposures?
        o   Example: Machine guarding, ventilation and extraction systems, wetting down
            techniques to minimise dust, mechanisation of process, etc.
       Using administrative guidelines, procedures, training etc., to minimise the impact of a
        hazard on a person.
        o   Consideration: Can safe work procedures be developed and implemented to minimise
            the risk? Can information, instruction and training be provided to help minimise the risk?
            Can shift roster arrangements limit the exposure?
        o   Example: For example, job rotation, safe operating procedures or lock out procedures.
       Personal Protective Clothing & Equipment (PPE) is the least effective means of
        controlling risks and can be expensive in the long term. PPE should not be considered
        unless all other control options have been exhausted. If PPE is to be considered it must be
        implemented as a program, which includes:-
        o   Appropriate selection of PPE;
        o   Training of personnel in correct use of PPE;


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                               Page | 119
        o   Maintenance of PPE; and
        o   Supervision to ensure correct use.
       It is important to assess if a control identified may introduce new hazards into the workplace.
        If so, Managers need to ask as to whether the control identified remains the preferred option
        and how any new risks are to be controlled.
       Finally, the manager needs to ensure that each further control is listed in the risk
        assessment document. The name/s of the person/s who will implement the controls
        together with time frames is also to be recorded in the risk assessment form.
       When managers decide on time frames for the implementation of controls they must
        consider both high risk and lower risk hazards when setting priorities.
       Some controls can easily be implemented or require little or no expense - these should be
        implemented quickly. For OHS corrective actions not able to be implemented immediately
        because they require more work, resources or funds, short term interim actions must be
        implemented to manage the risk in the interim.
       Managers to ensure that corrective actions identified are clearly written so they can be
        easily understood.
       Once corrective actions are implemented these should be signed off, and verified as
        complete in the register.
       If a risk is assessed and after all the controls identified /included, it remains a high and /or
        very high risk, a Management Plan (if not already in place) must be created. E.g. extra
        supervision, more frequent inspections or checks, etc.
Step 4 – Residual Risk rating
     Re-evaluate every risk once more (as per Section 4.4.e) taking into consideration all the
         controls and mitigation measures implemented. REMEMBER: Effective controls will reduce
         either likelihood, consequence or both.
       If the final Risk Rating (Residual risk rating) is equal to or above 22 (Extreme), the task may
        not be carried out until all controls are reviewed and the residual risk rating has been
        reduced to an acceptable level
Step 5 – Management of Change
     When a change may be required to the organisation, and/or the HSE/OHS Management
         System, and / or any activity / procedure, a comprehensive risk assessment shall be
         performed.
       The risk assessment shall be performed according to Steps 1 – 3.
       All potential and / or actual risks and hazards shall be identified.
       The change shall NOT be implemented BEFORE the risk assessment has been completed
        and the necessary controls identified / implemented so as to ensure the risk is at an
        accepted level.
Step 6 – Communication of Risks to all Employees.
     Supervisors must ensure every risk assessment is communicated with the relevant
         employee(s).
       Employees must write their name and sign as confirmation that the risk assessment has
        been discussed with them, and that they are acknowledging that they have been informed of
        the risks and appropriate preventive steps associated with an activity or task.
       Where possible make use of a translator to convey the message in one’s native language.


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 120
Step 7 – Review and Improve
     Step six of the risk management process involves reviewing and improving existing risk
         control systems.
       A systematic evaluation and review schedule will also identify potential new hazards as well
        as check how effective existing controls are.
       Other triggers for undertaking an risk assessment are if the workplace is:-
        o   Purchasing new or used equipment, hiring equipment, or using new substances and
            processes;
        o   Planning for the impact of new legislation;
        o   Responding to incident / hazard reports;
        o   Responding to issues raised by SHE Reps or others; and
        o   When new information becomes available regarding the nature of the hazard, or if new or
            more effective control methods become available or practicable.
HIRA Documentation
     Workplaces are required to develop internal documents and / or systems to record all
       corrective actions identified from the risk assessment process.
Overview
     Various types of risk assessments:
        o   Baseline RA (Eagle’s view on project)
        o   Task / issue-based RA: Conducted BEFORE task / activity
        o   Mini RA (Daily)
       Hierarchy of control:
        o   Eliminate,
        o   Substitution,
        o   Engineering controls,
        o   Signs/warnings and/or administrative controls,
        o   PPE
       Include risks to community, road crossings, water crossings (river, wetlands, etc.), bridges,
        transmission lines, pipelines, canals, tunnels, relocation of roads, etc.
       Occupational Health to be included.
       Include routine & non-routine work
       Include hazards created originating outside the workplace capable of adversely affecting
        workers
       Include hazards & risks that poses risks to or adverse impacts on the health and safety of
        affected communities.
       Include infrastructure, equipment and materials at the workplace
       Review
        o   After every high potential incident
        o   After every injury incident

       Baseline conducted by team of management including safety officer
       Task / Issue based conducted by a team (Safety officer, relevant supervisor, person(s)
        performing task, safety representative(s), specialists)

All risk assessments to be re-assessed after any change in conditions and for new or revised work methods, etc. These re-assessments to be handled and new revision numbers issued through the
document control system. If re-assessment does not yield any change in the risk, it shall be noted on the existing risk assessment form and signed by the Workplace Manager

                                                                                         IMPORTANT NOTICE
All risk assessments to be re-assessed after any change in conditions and for new or revised work methods, etc. These re-assessments to be handled and new revision numbers issued through the
document control system. If re-assessment does not yield any change in the risk, it shall be noted on the existing risk assessment form and signed by the Workplace Manager

STEP 5: REVIEW OF RISK ASSESSMENTS
Workplaces do not stay the same; sooner or later, you will bring in new equipment, substances and procedures that could lead to new hazards. One
should therefore review what you are doing on an on-going basis. Every second month, look at the risk assessment to make sure there is improvement and
back sliding? Are improvements still to be made? Have the workers spotted a problem? Have you learnt anything from incidents or near-hits? Make sure
your risk assessment stays up to date
At any time, if there is a significant change, do not wait; check the risk assessment and where necessary, amend it. If possible, it is best to think about the
risk assessment when you are planning your change - that way you leave yourself more flexibility. One should think about reviewing the risk assessment if:-
      new machinery or equipment are brought onto the worksite
      new processes or working practices are implements
      new substances are introduced on the site
      new personnel are hired (including sub-contractors)
      The workplace alters due to moves, severe weather conditions, expands rapidly, high impact changes, etc.
      the workplace experiences an incident or near-hit
Record the dote when risk assessments are reviewed; only alter records if there are new and significant findings or if existing precautions prove inadequate
or can be improved.

Indicative List of Procedures that May Be Required

     No                                     Description                                      Yes   No
      1   Hazard Identiﬁcation & Risk Assessment Procedure:
      2   Accident / Incident Reporting & Investigation Procedure:
          2.1 Reporting structure
          2.2 Responsible investigators (team)
          2.3 Must deﬁne the What, Who, When and How.
          2.4 Injury Register
          2.5 Incident Register
      3   Working at height. Procedure:
          1.1 All work performed where there is a risk of falling is classiﬁed as "Working
              at Height".
          1.2 Only double lanyard safety harnesses to be used on site.
          1.3 Employees to be trained in the correct use of a safety harness.
          1.4 All safety harnesses to be numbered, recorded in a register and inspected
              monthly by a competent person. Findings to be recorded in the register.
          1.5 Fall prevention always 1st option then fall protection.
          1.6 Drills must be conducted to "rescue" a worker hanging on a safety harness.
      4   Electrical & mechanical lock-out procedure:
          4.1 Lock-out (who, how and with what)
          4.2 Relevant persons trained. (competent electricians)
          4.3 Stored energy (hydraulics, pneumatics, compressed air, steam, electricity,
              springs, etc still under pressure) removed before work commence.
          4.4 Test before work.
          4.5 Who may remove lock in emergency?
          4.6 Key to be removed when operator / driver leaves machinery / plant / vehicle.
      5   Waste Disposal Procedure:
          5.1 There might be a need for a separate waste management plan.
          5.2 Comply with the minimum requirements as per the Tajikistan legislation.
          5.3 Hazardous waste to be disposed as prescribed in the relevant Material Safety
              Data Sheet.
      6   Management of Change Procedure:
          6.1 Change in methodology, material, equipment, design, etc.
          6.2 Baseline RA updated
          6.3 New Task/Issue Based RA
          6.4 New Method Statement
          6.5 Who must approve?
          6.6 Who must be informed?

APPENDIX 6: INJURIES AND INCIDENTS


The key safety definitions in this document have been developed from existing procedures, industry
best practice, relevant legislation, and from internationally recognized safety management system
standards, such as ISO 45001.
Application and Scope
The scope of these definitions covers all operational activities that have the potential to affect the
safety of employees, contractors, partners and visitors within the project. In order to ensure that the
same standard is used across all sites, the classification and calculation of illnesses and injury rates
will be according to the incident triangle below.




Acronyms



   Acronym              Explanation
   AI                   All Injuries
   AIFR                 All Injury Frequency Rate
   FAC                  First Aid Case
   HIPO                 High Potential Incident
   LTI                  Lost Time Injury
   LTIFR                Lost Time Injury Frequency Rate
   MTC / MTI            Medical Treatment Case / Medical Treatment Injury
   RI                   Recordable Injuries
   RIFR                 Recordable Injury Frequency Rate



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment

Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                               Page | 156

                    occupational diseases.

                    all injuries / illnesses which is     of hours worked by 100 employees in one year.

Classification
On site injuries
In principle, on site injuries are all regarded as company or work-related injuries with very few
exceptions. Once the employee enters the site boundary or working environment the working
relationship has been established and any injury, however caused, should be allocated to the
company. Thus, the basis for determining work relationship is that the event occurred in the work
environment.
Example 1: A clerk leaves the office and walks to one of the stores to visit a friend. On the way she
steps into a hole and injures her ankle. In this example she is on duty (and being paid by the
company) and the hole is on the company premises, therefore it is a company or work-related injury
(regardless of the fact that she was not doing her clerical work at the time).
The following exceptions are provided guidelines:
        At the time of the injury, the employee was present in the work environment as a member of
         the public rather than as an employee. An example would be an off-duty employee coming
         on-site to attend a union meeting.
        The injury involves signs or symptoms that surface at work but result solely from a non-
         work-related event (e.g., a previous football match).
        The injury results solely from voluntary participation in a wellness program, fitness program
         or recreational activity.
        The injury is solely the result of an employee eating or drinking. For example, if an
         employee is injured by choking on a sandwich while in the employer's establishment, the
         case would not be considered work-related.
        The injury is solely the result of an employee doing personal tasks (unrelated to their
         employment) at the establishment outside of the employer's assigned working hours.
        The injury is solely the result of personal grooming, self-medication for a non­work-related
         condition, or is intentionally self-inflicted.
        The injury is caused by a motor vehicle accident and occurs on an external company
         parking lot or company access road while the employee is commuting to or from work.
Off-site Injuries
There are two "rule of thumb" questions that govern the applicability of off-site Injuries. These are:
        Was the employee on duty at the time?
        Was the employee performing company tasks as part of his normal duties at the time?
Example 2: A fitter reports for duty at his workshop. During the morning a friend advises him that his
car in the external car park has a puncture. With permission, he proceeds to the car park to change
the wheel. During this activity the jack slips and the fitter is injured. In this example the fitter was on
duty at the time but was off-site performing private work. This is therefore not a company injury.
Example 3: A fitter is requested by his foreman to go off-site to fetch some spares. At the supplier
an argument occurs and the employee is assaulted. In this example the fitter was both on duty and


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 162
performing a company task when he was injured. It would therefore be recorded as a company
injury.
Injury allocation

In all cases employee injuries will be allocated to the injured person and the Business Unit for which
he/she works regardless of who is to blame for the incident i.e. there is to be no re-allocation of the
injury statistics. The Unit that employs the injured person will record the injury.
Transport injuries
The approach applies to the classification of transport related incidents:
       Injuries to employees transporting project products (typically in a Light Delivery Vehicle
        (LDV) or Heavy Delivery Vehicle (HDV) to and from a customer would be a work-related
        injury.
       Injuries to Service Provider employees (e.g. delivery of PPE) transporting project products
        or not, are not recorded once the vehicle is off-site except that the incident is recorded as a
        "Transport Incident". Note that we do not record the hours worked nor the injuries incurred
        and responsibility is taken by the Service Provider. However any injury incurred by a Service
        Provider transport employee on site should be recorded and the hours worked on site
        should be taken into account.
Assault on Security Employees
Injuries incurred by Security employees as a consequence of assault while carrying out their duties
are regarded as work-related injuries.
Bee Stings, Spider bites, Animal bites and Snake bites
Stings or bites that occur by chance from these sources and result in medical treatment are
regarded as Medical Cases. However, if an employee is instructed to remove bee's, animals or
snakes or to enter a sump where spiders are known to exist, then any resulting sting or bite would
be a work-related injury.
Car Parks (outside site boundaries)
Injuries that occur in car parks outside site boundaries are not work-related injuries even if the
parking area is provided by the company. However, if the employee is engaged in a work-related
activity in the car park, e.g. repairing car shelters, then it would be a work-related injury.
Car Parks (inside site boundaries)
Injuries that occur inside site boundaries are regarded as work-related injuries. (Note that the
working relationship has been established once the employee enters the site.)
Death as a result of complications during or after surgery.
If an employee dies as a result of complications during or after surgery which was necessitated and
directly related to a work-related injury or illness, then the case is recordable and classified as fatal.
If the underlying injury or illness was recorded prior to the employee's death, the employer must
update the information from its original classification to fatal.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                        October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                  Page | 163
Driving to and from Work
Injuries that occur when travelling to and from an employee's normal place of work are not work-
related injuries. However, if the Company provides the transport (e.g., a bus), then an injury incurred
would be work-related.
Injuries incurred by employees travelling to their normal place of work in response to a call­out are
regarded as being work-related. This also applies to the return journey. Any deviations undertaken
from the direct route home (e.g. shopping or sport activities) would negate the return journey.
Injuries that occur when driving directly from home to other sites or conference centres (e.g. Indaba
Hotel) are considered to be work-related. This driving is regarded as a business related activity. Any
deviations undertaken from the direct route home would negate the return journey. If the employee
is already at his/her place of work and then drives to another site/conference centre then any injury
incurred would also be regarded as a work-related injury, irrespective of whether the employee was
driving or a passenger in the vehicle.
Driving to multi-day Conferences

Injuries that occur when driving directly from office or home to a conference, and vice versa, are
considered to be work-related. This driving is regarded as a business related activity. Any deviations
undertaken from the direct route home (e.g. shopping or sport activities) would negate the return
journey. Any injury incurred during normal working hours while at the conference would also be
work-related. After hours the employee is deemed to be "home away from home" and any injury
would be regarded as not work-related.
If driving is necessary between the accommodation provided and the conference centre before the
work day commences or after it has ended, the accommodation is considered to be the "home away
from home". Therefore any injuries incurred during this driving activity would not be work-related.
Driving to Recognition Functions after normal working hours
Recognition functions are social functions where attendance is considered voluntary. Therefore any
injury incurred to and from the function as well as while at the function, would be regarded as not
being work-related. However the organiser of a function or any person who is expected to be
present (after normal working hours) would be regarded as being on duty and performing a
company task, therefore any injury incurred would be regarded as being work-related.
Fainting followed by an Injury
An employee fainting or collapsing while on duty would be a "Medical Case". When fainting or
collapsing the employee may get injured, e.g. hit his/her head resulting in a laceration requiring
stitches. Such an injury is regarded as consequential and is therefore not a work-related injury.
Heat- or Cold-Related Injuries
Heat stress that results from performing company tasks is classified as an Occupational Illness.
Heatstroke, sunburn and sunstroke also fall into this category. Similarly, frostbite and hypothermia
experienced during assigned tasks are considered Occupational Illnesses.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                               Page | 164
Hijacking Incidents
Post-traumatic stress that results from a vehicle hi-jacking incident whilst on duty is classified as an
Occupational Injury as it results from a single instantaneous event.
Horseplay at work
Injuries resulting from horseplay at work are regarded as work-related injuries.
Injuries on Sites where workers are on-site 24 hours a day
If a site has adopted a policy where employees or Service Providers have to reside within the site
boundaries then these people are deemed to be on duty all the time. Any injury sustained by these
employees would be deemed work-related irrespective of the circumstances.
Known Physical Defect (Pre-existing condition)
If an injury results solely from a known physical defect (i.e. employee falls while walking when a trick
knee gives way), it is not work-related. However, if the work environment or a work event contributes
(i.e. employee steps on a stone or slips, trick knee gives way, and he falls), any resulting injury
would be work-related.
Late Reporting of Injuries

Employees are required to report injury incidents as soon as possible but not later than the end of
the shift when the incident occurred. If this is not done, there must be adequate subsequent proof
that the incident occurred and adequate reason why the incident was not reported, for the injury to
be taken into account. If this is not available the injury will be regarded as not work-related.
Lunchtime Activities

Employees are not paid for the time they take off for their lunch break. Therefore, injuries that occur
during this period (e.g. if employees play cricket, soccer, volleyball, etc.) are not work-related
injuries. However, if the employee continues to work during his lunch break (and this can be shown
during the incident investigation) then an injury incurred would be work-related.
Marketing Activities e.g. Playing Golf
Any injury incurred by a marketing employee playing golf in an official capacity would be regarded
as a work-related injury. In all similar instances it must be established whether attendance at the
event was voluntary or whether the employee was on duty and performing a company task.


Injuries from voluntary attendance are considered as being not work-related. In the case of clients or
Service Providers attending such events, it is assumed that their attendance is voluntary; therefore
any injury is considered as being not work-related.
Overtime

Injuries that occur during authorized overtime are regarded as work-related injuries regardless of
whether the overtime is voluntary or under instruction, as long as company (and not private) tasks
are being undertaken.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 165
Personal grooming injury
Personal grooming activities are those directly related to personal hygiene, such as combing and
drying hair, brushing teeth, clipping fingernails, etc. Any injury incurred during these activities would
not be work-related.
Recreational Facilities

The definition of work premises excludes company controlled amenities and recreational facilities
(e.g. sport fields, tennis courts, golf courses, parks or game parks, swimming pools, gyms). These
facilities are usually apart from the workplace and are used by employees on a voluntary basis for
their own benefit outside of working hours. Therefore injuries in these facilities are not considered to
be work-related.
Restrooms, lunchrooms and cafeteria
Injuries that occur in these areas are considered to be work-related injuries unless they occur during
an official lunch break. These areas are considered to be part of the work environment for
employees who are normally stationed at these facilities (e.g. a cleaner working in the cafeteria).
Self-inflicted Injuries

Intentional self-inflicted injuries are not regarded as work-related injuries.
Site Boundaries (Workplace)

Site boundaries are determined by the doors, gates (booms), and/or pedestrian entrances at the
boundaries of the site. If an office worker twists his ankle inside the site it would be a work-related
injury. However, if the incident occurred outside the turnstile it would not be regarded as a work-
related injury. (Note that site boundaries must be established as they indicate when the working
relationship has commenced.)
Team Building Exercises
Any injury that occurs on a team building exercise during normal working hours is regarded as a
work-related injury. If employees are expected to be present at the events or participate in activities
then they are regarded as on duty.
Injuries that occur on team building exercises held outside normal working hours, which are
voluntary and employees were duly informed of this are regarded as not being work-related.
Visitors
In all cases their injuries must be taken into account and arrangements made to record (or estimate)
their applicable hours. Injuries must be allocated to the site visited when the injury occurred. It is
the responsibility of every project employee being visited to look after the safety of their visitors and,
where necessary, to make the required Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) available.
Violence (fighting or assault) at work
Injuries resulting from violence or assault at work are regarded as work-related injuries.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 166
Working at Home
If an employee voluntarily takes work home and is injured while working at home this is not regarded
as a work-related injury. However if the employee is paid or compensated for working at home and
the injury is directly related to the performance of the work, then it would be work-related.
Work Secondment

Injuries that are incurred by trainees (or other employees) when officially seconded to a particular
site will be allocated to the area where the employee is working at the time of the injury as the site is
responsible for his/her supervision. Injuries incurred by employees attending courses will be
regarded as work-related.

36. RESERVOIR LANDSLIDE MANAGEMENT PLAN
 INTRODUCTION
 The Tajik Government has established the Rogun Open Joint Stock Company (hereafter the ‘Rogun
 JSC’) to own and operate the Rogun Hydro Power Project (hereafter the ‘Project’), situated in
 central Tajikistan, at the eastern end of the Pamir Mountains. Separately, the Project Management
 Group (PMG) has been appointed to oversee construction of the Project.
 A lender consortium, led by the World Bank, collectively called the ‘Lenders’, are considering
 financing the Project. The Project has therefore been developed in accordance with the Lender’s
 policy and requirements.
 As part of the Project the requirement for a reservoir landslide management plan has been
 identified.
 The Project is being developed primarily in alignment with World Bank Environmental and Social
 Standards (ESS). ESS4: Community Health and Safety, Annex A Safety of Dams, describes World
 Bank’s approach to management of dam safety for new or existing dams. ESS4 indicates that a
 borrower needs to demonstrate GIIP to address dam safety issues, which is inferred to include
 reservoir landslides. Reservoir slope instability is typically considered as a dam safety issue and
 ESS4 references many internationally recognised documents providing guidance on risk
 management and dam safety.
 Report Purpose
 This report provides a framework for reservoir landslide risk mitigation and monitoring commitments
 that are required to ensure the Project remains compliant with international good practice. These
 commitments are derived from the outcomes of the Project Environmental and Social Impact
 Assessment (ESIA).
 This report is an outline document that will be updated periodically to reflect increased
 understanding of reservoir landslide risk affecting the Project. It will be informed by new studies as
 they become available and will also be updated based on the behaviour of slopes around the
 reservoir during reservoir filling and operation.
 This reservoir landslide management plan should be read in conjunction with Volume 2, A13.2:
 Reservoir Slope Stability of this ESIA.
 Ongoing Review
 This reservoir landslide management plan will be finalised prior to further raises of the reservoir. The
 management plan should be approved by the Dam Safety Manager and thereafter regularly
 reviewed and approved by the Dam Safety Panel of Experts (PoE) as part of the dam safety system
 for the Project.
 ROLES AND RESPONSIBILITIES
 A key component of the successful delivery of this reservoir landslide management plan is the full
 understanding of roles and responsibilities required under this plan. It is the responsibility of PMG to
 ensure that all relevant Project staff and contractors adhere to the requirements of the management

PMG will be responsible for ensuring the reservoir landslide management plan is updated, as
required, to ensure its efficacy as the Project develops, and that updates are submitted for approval

technical specialists will also need to be contracted to assist with investigation and assessment, with
such people answerable to the Project Design Manager.

emergency management plans will be with the Project Design Manager. Once the Project is
operational (i.e. after reservoir filling is completed) responsibility for reservoir landslide management
should be transferred to the Dam Safety Manager.

RESERVOIR LANDSLIDE RISK MANAGEMENT
This section provides details of the outline aims of this reservoir landslide management plan
together with objectives required to be met in order to deliver these aims. Specific risks associated
with reservoir landslides and mitigations are summarised in Table 2.2 and Table 2.3.
Aims and Objectives
The points below describe the key steps in developing an effective reservoir landslide management
plan.
Identify all existing landslides or currently stable slopes that could be affected by reservoir filling and
operation or could present a landslide dam risk to the Project.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                        October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                  Page | 171
Assess the expected performance of the above landslides and slopes following reservoir filling and
during operation.
Assess the potential triggers (rainfall, earthquake or elevated piezometric levels) for new or renewed
landslide movement that could affect the reservoir.
Evaluate the potential effect of landslide movement on the reservoir, area around the reservoir and
the dam.
Identify any remedial work that may be required to mitigate the effects of high hazard landslides or
slopes.
Where required develop a suitable monitoring programme to measure key parameters such as
deformation rate, piezometric levels, etc. The monitoring programme should be implemented as
soon as practical to collect historical baseline data that can be used to compare to any changes in
landslide behaviour during and after reservoir filling. The monitoring programme is important as a
method of identifying potential reduction in stability of landslides or slopes that could result in
imminent or future rapid movement.
Develop emergency preparedness plans and emergency action plans to ensure that potentially
affected communities can be alerted to the hazard and potentially evacuated if poor landslide
behaviour is detected.

MANAGEMENT ACTIONS
Introduction
This section presents the management actions and targets required to be deliver the aims and
objectives listed within Section 10.3. These actions are indicative at this stage and will be reviewed /
refined by PMG as part of the finalising reservoir landslide management plan (and to the approval of
the Lender’s TA).
Actions
A technical team, reporting to the design manager and subsequently the dam safety manager, shall
be given the task to install, maintain, collect data from the monitoring system and to prepare and
disseminate reports summarising the performance of the reservoir slopes, landslides and catchment
area.
Commence InSAR (Interferometric Synthetic Aperture Radar) monitoring (area wide analysis of
satellite imagery to measure ground movement) of the reservoir and catchment to review satellite


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 176
imagery available from previous years and identify any recent slope instability deformation in the
reservoir and catchment area and to provide an ongoing basis for identifying and monitoring future
movement.
Reconnaissance level engineering geological assessment of the entire reservoir and catchment to
identify existing landslides or slopes that could potentially develop into landslides in response to
reservoir filling or operation. This action has been partly completed and the findings incorporated in
Volume 2, A13.2: Reservoir Slope Stability of this ESIA.
Qualitative risk assessment of identified existing or potential landslides to prioritise detailed landslide
investigations. This action has been partly completed and the findings incorporated in Volume 2,
A13.2: Reservoir Slope Stability of this ESIA.
Implement a programme of investigations for potentially high hazard landslides that have not yet
been assessed in detail. The level of detail to be sufficient to quantitatively assess the risk posed by
each landslide to the Project, potentially affected communities and infrastructure and sensitive
environmental receptors.
Complete a Project wide quantitative risk assessment of the identified reservoir landslides and
slopes. This risk assessment should take into account the likelihood of potential trigger events and
the potential consequences of rapid movement of the landslide or slopes. This work should assess
whether engineering works are required to mitigate the risk from individual landslides.
Develop a landslide monitoring programme that can identify potential trigger conditions that could
initiate rapid movement of a slope or landslide. These triggers will include earthquake shaking,
rainfall (Project rain gauges and forecasts) and piezometric levels and potentially flow rates from any
installed drainage works. The monitoring programme must include alert values that signal behaviour
outside of the normal range and a system to promptly inform the responsible person so that
emergency actions can be commenced if necessary.
Using the risk assessment, develop a full Emergency Response and Preparedness Plan (ERPP)
(outline provided in Volume 3, B12 of this ESIA) plan that identifies safe evacuation routes and
muster areas in the event that a rapid landslide is considered imminent or occurs. The ERPP should
identify who is responsible for commencing emergency actions and have clear lines of
communication for communities and emergency services.
The actions and targets for each Aim and Objective are presented chronologically above in Table
36-3.
MONITORING
This section presents a summary of the provisional monitoring requirements required to be delivered
under the reservoir landslide management plan, together with the pathways to inform adaptive
management on the Project. This section will be finalised within the detailed reservoir landslide
management plan in order to ensure an effective monitoring strategy is adopted.
A comprehensive reservoir landslide monitoring programme will be developed to measure
deformation rates and other geotechnical parameters that are relevant to landslide performance.
Deformation monitoring will include InSAR analysis to identify creep deformation of any slopes
around the reservoir and catchment over the period of available historical data. On-going InSAR
monitoring as new data is available will also be undertaken to identify recent or current movement
trends. Conventional surveying, potentially including automated theodolites and permanent GPS


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 177
stations will be used to confirm any deformation trends and to provide real time deformation
monitoring as appropriate. Piezometers and subsurface instrumentation such as inclinometers will
be used to identify and monitor changes in ground water systems and deformation behaviour at
depth, as appropriate. Rain gauges will be monitored to evaluate and identify potential trigger level
high intensity rainfall events and to assess the correlation between rainfall and landslide behaviour.
Seismic monitoring stations will be installed to measure earthquake ground motions around the
reservoir.
REPORTING
Behaviour of the slopes around the reservoir, including landslides, measured by the monitoring
system will be reported to the dam design manager and when transition to Operation phase is
complete, to the dam safety manager. Measurements outside prescribed values for deformation
rates, groundwater levels, rainfall, ground motions, etc. will be reported in real time to allow
implementation of the emergency action plans if required. Routine regular summary reports of
landslide and slope behaviour will be disseminated to the Project owner, operator, panel of experts
and any applicable regulators to communicate landslide behaviour relative to expectations and allow
a basis for any changes in the monitoring system, reporting cadence or implementation of any
required physical mitigation works. Reservoir landslide monitoring reports will also contribute to
regular dam safety reviews.
ADAPTIVE MANAGEMENT
The observational approach to adaptive management of landslides is a commonly adopted
approach. This approach recognises that landslide mechanisms and behaviour are complex and the
subject of significant uncertainty. Therefore, regular review of monitoring information and landslide
behaviour should be undertaken and threshold values that trigger engineering or emergency
responses could be adjusted subject to rigorous review. The adaptive management approach relies
on adequate density and historical duration of monitoring data to allow informed management
decisions to be made. Because of the dam safety aspects of reservoir landslides, development of an
ERPP and subsequent implementation of the plan should be undertaken in consultation with
DSPoE. Once the Project is operational, responsibility for reservoir landslide management should be
assigned to the Dam Safety Manager.
CONCLUDING REMARKS
Reservoir slope stability is a key concern that would need attention during construction and
operation phases of the Project, with special attention needed during the initial impoundment. An
effective, long-term landslide management system—developed and implemented prior to lake filling
and continued throughout the reservoir’s operational life—should maintain landslide risk at
acceptable levels. Ongoing management and monitoring are essential to ensure that any remaining
risks remain minor and controlled. This approach aligns with international best practices (e.g.,
ICOLD Bulletin 124).
The residual impact of Impact waves inundating areas around the reservoir rim (including the dam),
and on Slope stability around the reservoir perimeter (affecting property and infrastructure) is
assessed as Minor, presuming diligent implementation of the mitigation measures, which include
ongoing investigation and data collection and reporting, risk assessment, and effective flood
management planning, including coordination with relevant authorities responsible for Emergency
Preparedness in the catchment and riparian areas.
