      Communication and Signalling: Operators and ground personnel must maintain clear
       communication. Flaggers should use visible signals and remain at a safe distance during
       operations.
Overhead Hazards And Height Restrictions
Overhead hazards, including power lines, tunnel roofs, construction equipment, and temporary
installations, pose significant risks to vehicle and equipment operations. Effective route planning and
work area risk assessments are essential to prevent collisions. Measures implemented on-site to
support contractors include:
      Signage and Indicators: Clearly mark overhead hazards, such as low-clearance zones and
       power lines. Install height indicators at critical points, including tunnel entrances and
       intersections, to guide operators effectively.
      Clearance Requirements: Establish and communicate minimum clearance thresholds for
       vehicles and equipment. Use barriers or gantries to enforce restrictions and prevent access
       to areas where height limits are exceeded.


TRANSPORT INFRASTRUCTURE DESIGN AND MAINTENANCE
Road Maintenance And Safety
The existing road network within the project must be maintained to ensure safe and efficient
operations under high-traffic and challenging environmental conditions. Maintenance efforts must
prioritise identifying and rectifying hazardous conditions that pose risks to vehicle operations,
workers, and equipment. A qualified and experienced engineer must oversee road maintenance
activities to ensure compliance with safety and functionality requirements.
Road Deficiencies Requiring Correction
The following road conditions must be managed to maintain operational safety and efficiency:
      Road Surface Hazards: Any potholes, ruts, uneven surfaces, or excessive dust generation
       that create unsafe driving conditions must be repaired.
      Traffic Segregation Deficiencies: Roads must be clearly marked to identify traffic lanes
       and to separate pedestrian and work areas. Where markings are faded or missing, they must
       be reinstated.
      Inadequate Drainage and Erosion Risks: Roads impacted by poor drainage, water
       accumulation, or erosion must be repaired and protected using appropriate drainage and
       slope stabilisation measures.
      Restricted or Hazardous Access Points: Roads that do not accommodate the safe
       passage of vehicles, particularly at intersections, sharp bends, or steep inclines, must be
       reviewed and corrective actions implemented (e.g., widening, barrier installation, or improved
       signage).
      Lack of Emergency and Alternative Access Routes: Where essential access routes are at
       risk of obstruction or excessive wear, suitable contingency plans must be in place to maintain
       traffic flow.
Maintenance And Inspection Requirements

Regular inspections and maintenance must be conducted by both contractors and the ER/PMC to
ensure road conditions remain safe for all site users. Contractors with operational control over


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                    October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                              Page | 208
specific areas, including construction lots encompassing roads, are responsible for maintaining
roads within their designated zones. Where contractor maintenance is not applicable or appropriate,
any road damage or safety concerns must be reported to the ER/PMC, who will coordinate
necessary repairs to ensure road safety and functionality. Maintenance actions required include:
      Timely Repairs: All potholes, debris accumulation, and damaged road surfaces must be
       addressed promptly to prevent accidents or further deterioration.
      Sealed Road Maintenance: Where applicable, resealing and patch repairs must be
       conducted as necessary to maintain stable, drivable surfaces.
      Unsealed Road Upkeep: Roads composed of gravel or compacted materials must be
       regularly regraded and replenished to ensure durability, especially in high-use areas.
      Drainage and Erosion Control: Culverts, ditches, and drainage channels must be
       maintained to prevent flooding, washouts and surface degradation.
Documentation And Records
All road design plans, maintenance schedules, and inspection records must be documented and
reviewed regularly. Updates to road layouts or features must be approved by a qualified engineer
and incorporated into the TMP.
Temporary And Permanent Bridges
The Rogun HPP infrastructure currently includes temporary bridge structures, such as the
Secherogh access bridge, providing critical transport links across challenging terrain and water
bodies.
Temporary Bridge Requirements
Temporary bridges serve as essential access points during the construction phase and must be
actively managed to ensure their safe operation. Rogun JSC, in coordination with the ER/PMC and
relevant contractors, is responsible for enforcing these requirements.
      Speed Limits and Weight Restrictions: The PMC must ensure that clear signage
       displaying speed and weight limits is installed and maintained at both bridge approaches.
       Contractors must ensure compliance with these restrictions for all vehicles using the bridges.

      Routine Structural Inspections: Temporary bridges must be inspected by designated
       engineers or maintenance teams at regular intervals and after heavy rainfall, seismic activity,
       or heightened traffic loads. The ER/PMC will oversee inspections, and any structural
       concerns must be reported immediately to Rogun JSC.
Access Control And Authorised Use:
Only authorised vehicles and personnel are permitted to use temporary bridges.
Signage
Effective and standardised signage is critical to managing traffic and pedestrian movements,
promoting safety, and ensuring efficient navigation across the Rogun HPP site.
Signage must meet specific standards to maintain visibility, consistency, and road user awareness.
Primary Functions Of Site Signage
Site signage supports the following key functions:


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                   October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                             Page | 209
      Traffic Control: Regulates traffic flow and enforces rules at intersections, tunnels, and high-
       traffic zones.
      Hazard Awareness: Alerts road users to specific risks, such as environmental hazards or
       restricted areas.
      Directional Guidance: Ensures seamless navigation for vehicles and pedestrians through
       construction and operational zones.
Types Of Signage

Based on industry best practices and the specific conditions of the Rogun HPP site, signage is
categorised into three primary types:
Regulatory and Control Signage: These signs manage traffic flow, enforce rules, and maintain
safe operations in high-activity areas. Examples include:
      Intersection Control Signs: Installed at four-way intersections, such as near material
       handling and crushing plants, to manage multi-directional traffic.
      Speed Limit Signs: Standardised across the site to ensure consistent speed control and
       driver safety.
      Stop and Give-Way Signs: Positioned at junctions to maintain controlled traffic flow.
      Access Restriction Signs: "No-Go Zone" signage prevents unauthorised access to
       hazardous or restricted areas.
      Directional Indicators: One-way or two-way traffic signs, particularly in confined spaces like
       tunnels and bridges.
Hazard Warning Signage: Warning signs alert drivers and pedestrians to site-specific hazards.
Examples include:
      Pedestrian Pathways and Crossings: Clearly marked routes and crossings minimise
       vehicle-pedestrian interactions.
      Rockfall and Debris Warnings: Positioned near steep embankments and tunnels to
       highlight potential rockfall areas.
      Safe Distance Markers: Proximity signs (e.g., "40 m") on steep roads to minimise collision
       risks.
      Animal Crossing Signs: Installed in areas where cattle or wildlife are commonly present to
       reduce animal-vehicle incidents.
      Slippery Surface Warnings: Signs near slippery areas, such as tunnel entrances or water
       zones, advising reduced speeds.
Informational and Pedestrian Signage: These signs provide guidance for both vehicle operators
and pedestrians, supporting organised site operations and safety:
      Pedestrian Crossings and Pathways: Clearly marked pedestrian routes, walkways, and
       crossing points to guide foot traffic, minimise vehicle interactions, and enhance site safety.
      Parking and Drop-Off Zones: Signage for designated parking and shuttle drop-off points
       helps reduce congestion.
      Zone and Tunnel Identification: Signs indicating specific areas, tunnel numbers (e.g., "T-
       5"), and construction zones assist navigation.

      Contractor Boundary and Information Signs: Mark contractor-specific zones, boundaries,
       and contact details to clarify responsibilities and restrict unauthorised access.
Signage Implementation And Responsibility

Permanent Signage Responsibility: Permanent signage installation will take place upon
completion of construction. The ER/PMC will be responsible for ensuring signage is in place before
Contractors demobilize. parties responsible
Temporary Signage Responsibility: Temporary signage refers to all signs installed during
construction to communicate hazards, traffic control measures, and operational restrictions. These
signs are non-permanent and adaptable, remaining in place only for the duration of the relevant
work activities.
Temporary signage must be installed, maintained, and removed by contractors as site conditions
evolve. Signage must be documented in contractor TMPs and submitted for approval to the
Employer’s Representative/PMC before implementation.
Timeframe for Temporary Signage:
      During Construction: Temporary signs remain in place only while the associated works or
       hazards are present.
      Transition to Permanent Signage: As construction phases are completed and areas
       transition to operational status, permanent signage must be installed before handover or
       commissioning. The timeline for permanent signage will be confirmed in coordination with
       Rogun JSC and Project stakeholders.
Contractors must conduct regular inspections to ensure temporary signage remains visible, legible,
and effective. The PMC and Employer’s Representative will oversee site-wide consistency and
compliance with approved TMPs.
Placement And Installation
Signage must be strategically positioned to provide adequate warning and guidance to drivers and
pedestrians, particularly in areas with high traffic, restricted visibility, or complex intersections.
The PMC and the Employer’s Representative are responsible for reviewing and approving signage
placement to ensure alignment with traffic safety requirements. Contractors must install and
maintain signage within their designated work areas, conducting regular inspections to verify
visibility and compliance. The PMC will oversee site-wide inspections to identify any deficiencies and
ensure corrective actions are implemented.
Placement considerations include:
      Proximity to Intersections and High-Risk Areas: Signs must be placed in advance of
       intersections, pedestrian crossings, and sharp curves, allowing adequate response time.
      Illumination and Reflectivity: In areas with low visibility or night operations, signage must
       incorporate reflective materials or be illuminated to ensure readability.
      Clearance and Height Requirements: Signs must be installed at heights and angles that
       avoid obstructing sightlines while ensuring visibility for drivers and pedestrians.

Maintenance And Inspection
Both permanent and temporary signage must undergo regular inspections to ensure clarity, legibility,
and physical integrity. Contractors are responsible for maintaining temporary signage within their

Damaged, obscured, or worn signage must be promptly repaired or replaced to maintain high safety
standards.
Temporary And Project-Specific Signage

Adaptability for Changing Conditions: Temporary signage is essential in areas with dynamic traffic
patterns or changing road conditions, such as temporary access restrictions, road closures, and
heavy equipment operations. Examples include:
      Construction Zones: Temporary signs around active construction areas to warn of potential
       hazards, such as falling objects or heavy machinery or workers on foot.
      Oversized Load Indicators: Signs indicating weight and size restrictions on bridges or
       narrow paths to guide the movement of large vehicles.
Language And Comprehensibility
To ensure accessibility for all personnel, signage should be displayed in relevant languages,
including Tajik, English and Russian. Where possible, signs should use symbols or universally
recognised icons to enhance comprehension across language barriers.
Road Markings And Visual Aids
Road markings should complement signage to reinforce instructions, especially at intersections,
designated walkways, and blind spots. Mirrors should also be installed in locations with restricted
views, such as ramps and sharp curves, to enhance visibility and reduce the risk of collisions.
Signage Surveys And Updates

Regular signage surveys must be conducted by the Contractors and the ER/PMC to assess
placement effectiveness, identify deficiencies, and implement necessary updates. These surveys
must evaluate visibility, placement accuracy, and compliance with site traffic management
requirements.
As site conditions evolve, the ER/PMC must review and revise signage placement plans periodically
to ensure alignment with project demands, road layout changes, and updated safety protocols.
Contractors must implement required updates within their designated work areas to maintain
compliance.
Safety Barriers And Edge Protection
The Project site’s challenging terrain and steep roadways require safety barriers and edge
protection to prevent vehicle incidents. Measures such as safety barriers and edge protection
structures mitigate risks on haul roads and near cliff edges, addressing rollovers, loss of control, and
head-on collisions.
Contractors must assess site roads, high-risk areas, and traffic routes to determine where edge
protection and barriers are necessary. Based on site conditions, contractors must propose suitable
protective measures and submit them in their Contractor’s TMPs for approval by the PMC.

Contractors must consider the following control measures where applicable:
      Edge Protection for Haul Roads: Contractors must identify haul road sections, steep
       inclines, cliffs, parking, and staging areas where windows or earthen barriers are required.
       These barriers must be designed to withstand impacts from the heaviest vehicles on-site and
       be inspected regularly to ensure stability.
      Concrete Barriers in High Risk Areas: Where earthen berms are insufficient, such as
       along cliff edges and high-altitude routes, contractors must assess whether concrete barriers
       or equivalent protective measures are necessary based on site-specific risks.
      Barriers on Sharp Curves: Contractors must evaluate sharp curves and high-risk bends to
       determine whether containment measures, such as earth berms or concrete blocks are
       needed. Placement should consider vehicle speeds, road width, and traffic volume to ensure
       effectiveness.
      Lane Delineation in Narrow Zones: Where site conditions pose a significant collision risk,
       such as on narrow haul roads with two-way traffic, contractors may propose delineation
       measures in particularly hazardous areas where space allows. These may include flexible
       bollards, modular barriers, or other appropriate traffic separation measures.
Barriers and edge protections must be inspected routinely for damage, erosion, and stability, with
heightened attention following adverse weather conditions.
The Employer’s Representative, PMC and/or Safety Department are responsible for coordinating
barrier installation and conducting inspections of safety barriers and edge protection. Contractors
must report deficiencies immediately and implement corrective measures as required.
Hazard Markers And Mirrors
Strategic placement of hazard markers and mirrors improves visibility, reduces collision risks, and
enhances situational awareness across the site.
      Reflective Markers: Install reflective markers on key road sections, particularly in tunnels,
       dark areas, and high-risk zones, to guide vehicle movement and delineate road boundaries
       in low-light conditions.
      Traffic Mirrors: Place mirrors at obstructed intersections, tunnel exits, haul road crossings,
       sharp curves, and blind corners to improve driver visibility and reduce collision risks.
Physical Speed Reduction And Traffic Calming
Speed bumps should be installed in high-pedestrian activity zones, such as shuttle bus drop-off
areas, worker waiting zones, and intersections near material loading points. These installations
naturally enforce speed reduction, reducing collision risks and enhancing navigation safety.
Rumble strips may also be placed on approaches to critical areas, including intersections, sharp
curves, and tunnel entrances, to alert drivers and encourage gradual speed reduction.
Parking And Vehicle Staging
To ensure safety and reduce congestion, the Traffic Safety Section, Employer’s Representative, and
contractors must implement and manage the following parking and vehicle staging measures:
      Reverse Parking Protocol: Reverse parking is required across all site parking zones to
       enhance visibility when exiting and reduce collision risks with pedestrians and vehicles.

      Dedicated Parking Zones: Parking areas must be assigned for heavy equipment, site
       vehicles, shuttle buses, and personal vehicles, strategically located to minimise congestion
       and vehicle interactions.
      Markings, Signage, and Lighting: All parking areas must have clear ground markings,
       signage, and physical barriers. Adequate lighting must be provided, particularly in zones

      Contractor and Visitor Parking: Separate parking zones must be established for

      Staging for Oversized and Heavy Vehicles: Specific staging areas must be established for

      Loading and Unloading Zones: Dedicated loading and unloading zones must be allocated
       near work areas, marked with clear signage and ground markings. These areas must have
       barriers or physical controls to restrict pedestrian access and minimise interference with
       general traffic flow.
Dust Control Measures
Effective dust control is critical to maintaining safe visibility on site roads. To address this,
Contractors should deploy water trucks at a frequency aligned with the requirements set out in
Volume 1, Chapter 8: Air Quality, particularly along high-traffic routes and areas prone to dust
generation, especially during dry conditions in summer.
Washing Of Vehicles And Plant
Vehicle and plant washing stations should be established in designated areas equipped with mud
traps, fuel and lubricant traps, and drainage systems to manage wastewater safely. These stations
prevent the spread of mud, dust, and debris onto main traffic routes, improving road conditions and
reducing visibility hazards. All vehicle washing activities must comply with the requirements set out
in Volume 1, Chapter 13: Water, ensuring appropriate management of wastewater and
contamination risks.
Washing procedures should follow environmental and safety protocols, ensuring that used oils and
technical fluids are drained into appropriate containers for recycling or safe disposal. Contractors
must ensure that washing stations are regularly maintained, and all waste materials from vehicle
washing, such as oil products and operational fluids, are collected and stored in designated areas
for subsequent processing.


TRAFFIC REGULATION AND OPERATIONAL PROCEDURES
Speed Limits And Enforcement
The current speed limits for the Rogun HPP, as outlined in the current TMP, are as follows:
      Tunnel Traffic: 20 km/h
      Site Roads: 40 km/h




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                     October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                               Page | 214
These will remain applicable, and the risk assessment process Contractors’ implement in developing
their TMPs will identify areas where other limits are necessary. All such areas will be clearly posted
with appropriate signage. All vehicles must comply with the designated speed limits..
All drivers should be trained to reduce their speed in response to specific road and weather
conditions, particularly when visibility is limited, surfaces are slippery, sharp corners or narrow roads
are present.
Determining And Adjusting Speed Limits

Speed limits must be established based on a comprehensive, site-wide risk assessment, ensuring
they are appropriate for specific road and operational conditions. The following limits and
considerations must be applied:
      Road Conditions: Speed limits must be reduced on rough or narrow roads, sharp bends, and
       areas with limited visibility to minimise collision risks.
      Traffic Flow and Volume: Primary access routes must have standardised speed limits, while
       routes with high-traffic volumes or two-way traffic, must be assessed to determine if lower
       limits are necessary to manage flow and reduce overtaking risks.
      Vehicle Type: Heavy Goods Vehicles (HGVs) and construction equipment must operate
       under designated speed limits based on stopping distances and operational requirements.
      Pedestrian Areas: Speed limits must be reduced in high-pedestrian zones, including pick-
       up/drop-off areas, entrances, and designated crossings.
      Active Construction Zones: Construction zones must have reduced speed limits, accounting
       for increased risk in areas with simultaneous operations and proximity to heavy equipment.
      Right of Way Considerations: Speed limits must be set to prioritise traffic flow and prevent
       congestion, particularly for heavy vehicles at intersections and access points.
      Temporary Conditions: Temporary routes and detours must have lower speed limits to
       protect workers near active construction.
      Oversized Loads: Vehicles transporting oversized loads must adhere to lower speed limits to
       accommodate extended stopping distances and designated routes.
      Environmental Factors: Speed limits must be adjusted based on weather conditions, poor
       visibility (e.g., tunnels), and road hazards such as rockfalls or water pooling.
Consultation And Interface Management
Speed limits across the site must be determined and reconsidered with every review and update of
the TMPs, including this one, through a structured consultation process involving key stakeholders.
This process ensures that speed limits remain safe, practical, and aligned with operational
requirements.
The following stakeholders should be involved in the review process:
      Project Contractor Managers: Responsible for identifying area-specific risks, road conditions,
       and traffic patterns within their operational zones.
      Employer’s Representative and Safety Managers: Oversee compliance, conduct safety
       audits, review incident reports, and provide recommendations for speed limit adjustments.
      Traffic Safety Section and PMC: Ensure speed limits align with overall site traffic
       management, security measures, and access control protocols.

Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 215
      Worker Representatives: Provide feedback on the practicality of speed limits and highlight
       any recurring safety concerns encountered during daily operations.
Contractors are responsible for monitoring speed limits within their designated areas and the
ER/PMC and Traffic Department in all areas. They will identify areas where adjustments may be
necessary.
      Contractors must document speed-related incidents or concerns and submit reports to the
       PMC and ER as part of ongoing site safety management.
      Contractors may propose speed limit modifications based on observed traffic conditions or
       worker feedback. Any proposed changes must be submitted to the PMC/ ER for review and
       approval to ensure alignment with site-wide traffic safety standards.
Communication And Signage
Clear communication of speed limits is essential to maintaining compliance and safety across the
site. Speed limits must be reinforced through the following methods:
      Speed Limit Signage: Highly visible speed limit signs must be installed at key locations,
       including entry points, tunnels, intersections, and other critical areas ensuring drivers are
       aware of speed limits at all times.
      TMPs: Speed limits must be documented in each contractor’s TMP and clearly marked on
       traffic diagrams, for quick reference by site personnel.
      Site Inductions and Briefings: All personnel, including contractors, delivery drivers, and
       subcontractors, must be informed of speed limits during site inductions and regular briefings.
      Temporary Adjustments: Any temporary speed limit changes due to road maintenance,
       weather, or other site conditions must be communicated immediately through updated
       signage and contractor briefings.
      Speed Governors for Site-Based Plant: Where feasible, speed governors should be installed
       on mobile plant that does not travel off-site as an additional measure to control vehicle
       speeds within the construction area.
Speed Compliance And Enforcement
Speed limit compliance must be actively enforced to prevent accidents and mitigate risks associated
with incentive-driven driving behaviours, such as those arising from load-based remuneration
schemes. Contractors and site management must implement the following measures to ensure strict
adherence to speed limits:
      Speed Restrictors: Where feasible, vehicles assigned to load-based or high-output tasks
       should be fitted with speed restrictors to prevent speeding and reinforce safe driving
       behaviour.
      Contractor Remuneration Structures: Contractors must ensure that remuneration schemes
       prioritise safe driving over productivity-based incentives. Where feasible, alternative models
       that discourage speeding and reward adherence to safety protocols must be implemented.
      Physical Speed Controls: Speed humps, barriers, and other physical controls, as outlined in
       Section 9, must be installed in high-risk areas such as pedestrian pathways and site access
       points to enforce compliance.
      Supervisory Oversight: Supervisors must routinely monitor speed compliance within their
       designated areas, record instances of speeding, and report any violations to the PMC and
       ER.

      Penalties for Violations: Documented speed limit violations must result in appropriate
       disciplinary actions, particularly for repeat offenders, to reinforce compliance and deter
       unsafe driving practices.
Minimum Mandatory Safety Rules For Drivers And Operators
General Rules

The following safety rules are mandatory for all drivers and vehicle operators on-site to ensure
compliance, minimise risks, and maintain operational safety:
      Seatbelt Use: Drivers and passengers must wear seatbelts at all times. Functional checks
       must be conducted during pre-start inspections. Seatbelt bypasses or fobs are strictly
       prohibited, and any defects must be reported immediately.
      Prohibition of Mobile Device Use: The use of mobile phones or handheld devices while
       driving is prohibited, except for hands-free communication. Drivers must remain focused and
       distraction-free.
      Strict Speed Compliance: Drivers must adhere to posted speed limits. Violations will be
       monitored through inspections and speed checks, with disciplinary actions for non-
       compliance (refer to Section 9.1, Speed Compliance and Enforcement).
      Alcohol and Drug Prohibition: Operating a vehicle under the influence of alcohol or drugs is
       strictly prohibited.
      Overtaking: Overtaking is strictly prohibited in tunnels, except in designated areas where
       explicitly permitted. Drivers must adhere to designated routes and right-of-way rules to
       prevent unsafe manoeuvres.
      Headlight Use: Headlights must remain on at all times, including daylight hours, to enhance
       visibility in tunnels and dimly lit areas.
      Defensive Driving: Drivers must practice defensive driving, particularly in pedestrian areas,
       intersections, and high-traffic zones.
Operational Protocols
The following operational protocols must be followed to ensure safe vehicle use on-site:
      Pre-Start Checks: As noted above, all vehicles must undergo pre-start inspections by the
       operator covering brakes, lights, seatbelts, and safety features. Deficiencies must be
       addressed and approved by a Supervisor before operation.
      Vehicle Identification: Also as noted above, vehicles must display visible identification to
       allow tracking and reporting of to the responsible contractor and the ER/PMC.
      Communication Protocols: Drivers must use suitable communication methods with other road
       users to confirm actions at tunnels and intersections, ensuring coordinated movements (refer
       to Section 10.3 – Traffic Communication and Coordination).
      Stationary Vehicle Protocols: Stopped vehicles in tunnels or high-traffic areas must activate
       hazard lights and deploy additional warning signals, such as reflective markers or cones,
       where appropriate.
Compliance And Accountability

A standardised, enforceable framework for driver behaviour and road safety is required across the
site to ensure consistent compliance and protect all personnel. The following measures define
responsibilities and enforcement mechanisms

      Management Oversight: The ER/PMC, the Traffic Department, and the Contractors
       themselves are responsible for ensuring enforcement of road safety compliance across the
       site.
      Driver Training and Competency: All drivers must complete mandatory site-specific road
       safety training and demonstrate competency before being authorised to operate vehicles on-
       site. The PMC and Contractors are responsible for delivering this training (refer to Section 15
       – Training Requirements).
      Routine Compliance Checks: HSE specialists, supervisors, and managers from all
       contractors are responsible for conducting random compliance checks, including speed
       monitoring, seatbelt use, driver authorizations, and adherence to pre-start checklists and
       other safety requirements. Non-compliance must be documented and reported to the
       Employer’s Representative or PMC for corrective action. In addition, the ER/PMC and JSC
       Rogun will also conduct periodic checks and occasional focused inspections/audits.
      Routine Observations. Senior managers of all parties will be expected to be alert and take
       action if they observe violations of TMP requirements. Such actions could include stopping
       the vehicle and admonishing the driver and/or or reporting to the ER/PMC and the Contractor
       HSE manager. In addition, Contractors will be expected to report violations by other
       Contractors’ vehicles to the ER/PMC.
      Disciplinary Actions: Violations must be addressed through a structured disciplinary process,
       ranging from verbal and written warnings to suspension or removal from site for repeated or
       high-risk offences, such as reckless driving or unauthorised mobile device use. Vehicles
       without proper safety equipment, as listed above, are to be removed from service
       immediately and not returned until they have been repaired and approved by the Contractor’s
       HSE manager, an ER/PMC HSE specialist, or the Traffic Department.
Communication Protocols
Clear and consistent communication is required for all vehicle operations, especially in high-risk or
confined areas, to prevent miscommunication and ensure coordinated traffic movements.
Contractors must ensure that all drivers and equipment operators are trained in site-specific
communication protocols.
      Two-Way Radios: Where functional, two-way radios must be used in tunnels, intersections,
       and confined areas. Contractors must verify radio coverage within tunnels and, if unreliable,
       implement alternative communication methods such as designated signallers or hardwired
       communication systems where available.
      Standardised Signals: A set of defined hand signals, audible alerts (e.g., horns), and light
       signals should be used for critical actions, such as stopping, advancing, or indicating a
       hazard. These signals should be documented and included in site-wide training for all vehicle
       operators.
      Multilingual Considerations: Contractors must provide training on communication protocols,
       ensuring all drivers understand key terms, signals, and instructions, particularly given the
       diversity of on-site personnel.
      Mobile Device Restrictions: Drivers may only use hands-free devices for work-related
       communication while operating vehicles.
      Training Requirements: Communication protocols must be formally integrated into contractor
       training programs to ensure alignment with site-wide traffic safety standards.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                    October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                              Page | 218
Tunnel Traffic Management
Two-Way Traffic Operations
All tunnels on-site operate as two-way at all times. To manage traffic safely in confined tunnel
spaces, contractors must implement the following controls:
       Designated Passing Points: Clearly marked passing areas must be established within
        tunnels where space allows to enable vehicles to pass safely without congestion.
       Right-of-Way Rules: In tunnels, loaded haul trucks and heavy vehicles have right of way over
        lighter vehicles. Lighter vehicles must yield and wait at designated passing points where
        necessary to prevent disruptions and reduce collision risks.
       Communication in Tunnels: Drivers must use approved communication methods to
        coordinate movements, particularly at intersections, entry/exit points, or narrow sections.
        Where two-way radios are unreliable, alternative communication measures must be used,
        such as designated signallers or controlled access points.
       Stationary Vehicle Protocols: Vehicles stopping within tunnels must activate hazard lights
        and deploy warning signals, such as cones or reflective markers, to alert other drivers.
       Vehicle Visibility: All vehicles must keep beacons and headlights on at all times while
        operating in tunnels to improve visibility in low-light conditions.
       Dust Control and Air Quality Measures: Windows must remain closed to protect drivers from
        dust and airborne hazards. Vehicles operating in high-dust or low-ventilation areas must be
        equipped with cabin air filtration systems where required.
       Overtaking Prohibition: Overtaking is strictly prohibited within tunnels.
       Emergency Procedures: All personnel must be trained in tunnel-specific emergency
        response protocols, including evacuation routes, fire suppression measures, and vehicle
        breakdown procedures.
Traffic Lights – Entry, Exit, And Intersection Control
A mobile, automated traffic light system must be implemented at tunnel entry and exit points to
regulate vehicle movement and enhance safety.
       Traffic Light System: Portable, adaptable traffic lights must be used to manage vehicle entry
        and exit, adjusting to tunnel operations as required.
       Flow Regulation: Automated traffic lights must be paired with clear entry and exit protocols to
        control traffic flow, reduce congestion, and prevent overtaking within tunnels.
Manual Signalling And Flagger Roles
Flaggers must be deployed at key locations to regulate traffic movement and enforce right-of-way
rules.
       Flagger Requirements: Flaggers should be used for emergency scenarios or peak traffic
        periods where manual control is necessary. Flaggers should be stationed at designated high-
        risk locations, including tunnel intersections or where visibility is restricted.
       Communication and Coordination: Flaggers must be equipped with radios to coordinate with
        other control points across the tunnel network and communicate with drivers as needed.
       Placement in Safe Zones: Flaggers must be stationed in well-lit, ventilated areas and wear
        full PPE to mitigate exposure to hazardous conditions.



Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                    October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                              Page | 219
      Right-of-Way Rules: At tunnel intersections, vehicles from tunnel T5 have right-of-way when
       entering tunnel T3, and vehicles in T3 have right-of-way over those entering from tunnel T2.
       For all other intersections, contractors must establish site-specific right-of-way rules and
       submit them in their TMPs for approval by the ER/PMC.
Flaggers should be phased out where possible in favour of automated systems, reducing reliance on
personnel in hazardous environments and streamlining traffic control in tunnels.
Right Of Way Rules
Clear right-of-way (ROW) rules must be established and enforced to manage tunnel traffic safely,
particularly at intersections and narrow sections. Contractors must integrate these ROW rules into
their TMPs.
      Heavy Vehicle Priority: Haul trucks and other heavy vehicles have ROW over lighter vehicles
       to minimise disruptions and reduce collision risks in confined spaces.
      Primary Tunnel Priority: Vehicles traveling through main tunnels have ROW over those
       entering from secondary or side tunnels to prevent unnecessary reversing or unsafe
       manoeuvres.
      Staging Areas for Yielding Vehicles: Where physically possible, designated staging or waiting
       areas must be provided for vehicles required to yield ROW to maintain clear traffic flow in
       high-risk sections.
      ROW Enforcement via Automated Signals: Where automated traffic lights are installed, ROW
       rules must be programmed into the signal system to ensure consistent enforcement at tunnel
       intersections.
Ground Personnel Safety In Tunnels
The presence of ground personnel in tunnels must be strictly controlled due to confined spaces,
reduced visibility, and high vehicle traffic. All work zones must be clearly marked to ensure
personnel safety and separation from vehicle operations.
      Restricted Access: Access to active tunnel is limited to authorised personnel performing
       documented tasks. Non-essential foot traffic is prohibited to minimise risk.
      Mandatory Personal Safety Gear: Personnel must wear high-visibility PPE, to enhance
       visibility. They must also be provided with respiratory protection in tunnels where they are
       working more than 0.5km from an opening. .
      Designated Work Zones: All work zones must be clearly marked to ensure personnel safety
       and separation from vehicle operations.
      Maintaining Safe Distances: A minimum clearance distance from vehicles, including broken-
       down vehicles, must be maintained at all times.
Managing Peak Traffic Times And Congestion
This section addresses managing traffic flow during peak times, such as shift changes and lunch
breaks, to reduce congestion and improve safety and efficiency. These measures are designed for
the high-traffic construction phase, with the expectation that traffic volumes will decline significantly
once the project reaches operational capacity. The congestion management strategies outlined here
can therefore be phased out or adjusted as site traffic reduces over time.




Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 220
Congestion Hazards And Risks
Peak traffic periods on-site can create congestion, increasing the likelihood of unsafe driving
behaviours such as overtaking in tunnels, mobile phone usage, and driver inattention. Congestion
also contributes to operational delays, vehicle collisions, and heightened pedestrian risks. Managing
these periods effectively is essential to maintaining a safe and efficient work environment.
Key congestion periods include:
      Shift Changes: The busiest times occur at 6-7 am and 6-7 pm, coinciding with the 12-hour
       shift structure and major pedestrian movements during the twice-monthly shift rotations.
       These changeovers also align with local school traffic, creating additional off-site congestion
       risks.
      Lunch Breaks: Heavy traffic is observed around 11 am and 11 pm during site-wide breaks,
       with noticeable traffic reduction at noon when most workers are at lunch.
To reduce congestion and pedestrian risks, the ER/PMC and Contractors must investigate the
feasibility of implementing dedicated buses or personnel carriers for worker transport during peak
periods, particularly during the twice-monthly shift changes. Contractors are responsible for
managing the movement of their workers to and from site, and on site, ensuring compliance with
congestion control measures and minimising pedestrian risks.
Shift Scheduling Adjustments
Managing traffic flow effectively is essential to reducing congestion and minimising safety risks
during peak travel periods. Contractors should implement sufficient traffic management measures to
control worker movement and reduce vehicle clustering at site access points.
      Staggered Departures for Contractor Transport: Where feasible, contractor-operated
       transport services should be scheduled to reduce congestion at site exits during peak times.
      Traffic Control Measures at Entry and Exit Points: Flaggers, temporary signals, or other
       control measures should be used during high-traffic periods to ensure an orderly flow of
       vehicles.
      Pedestrian Route Management: Contractors should establish designated pedestrian
       pathways and crossings to separate foot traffic from vehicle movement, particularly during
       shift changes.
Traffic Volume Data Capturing And Analysis
To support initial preparation and subsequent reviews of their TMPs, Contractors must conduct
traffic volume monitoring to assess road usage patterns and identify high-risk areas. This data must
be used to inform congestion control measures, enhance route planning and improve overall traffic
safety.
      Traffic Counts and Data Collection: Conduct manual or electronic traffic counts at site entry
       and exit points, as well as at high-use intersections, to track vehicle movements, identify
       peak congestion times, and assess heavy-use areas.
      Identification of High-Risk Areas: Traffic volume monitoring is to be used to support the
       identification of hazardous zones, heavy-use roads, and critical congestion points.

Automated Traffic Light Systems
Automated traffic light systems enhance traffic regulation and flow in high-demand areas,
particularly during peak times or in confined zones.
Recommended applications:
      Site Entry Points and Intersections: Automated traffic lights should be deployed at site entry
       points, busy intersections, and contractor access zones to manage vehicle flow and minimise
       congestion. These systems may be mobile and adaptable to accommodate evolving site
       layouts and construction phases.
      Tunnel Entry and Intersection Controls: Automated traffic lights should be placed at tunnel
       entry/exit points and key intersections, as outlined in Section 10.4.
Traffic Planning And Scheduling For Construction Activities
Effective traffic planning and scheduling are essential to ensure safe, efficient movement across the
Rogun HPP site. Given the ongoing changes in construction activities, traffic routes, and
environmental factors, close coordination is required to prevent conflicts, minimise congestion, and
support safe and uninterrupted operations. This section outlines high-level responsibilities and
procedures to align construction traffic with the dynamic site environment. Contractor TMPs will
need to provide details of their own programs.
Coordinated Scheduling Of Traffic-Related Activities

To facilitate smooth traffic flow and prevent disruptions, contractors must coordinate traffic
schedules, taking into account construction activities, shift changes, and periods of high traffic
demand. The ER/ PMC and contractor managers are jointly responsible for aligning schedules with
traffic-related activities to avoid potential conflicts on key routes.
Key coordination activities:
      Weekly Planning Meetings: The PMC, Traffic Department, and contractor HSE managers
       must participate in weekly planning meetings to assess upcoming activities, anticipated traffic
       volume, and site conditions. These meetings will ensure coordinated traffic planning across
       all active work areas. As needed, senior managers may need to participate as well.
      Advanced Notification of Major Movements: Any contractor planning to move oversized
       loads, initiate road closures, or perform maintenance on key routes must notify all parties in
       advance to facilitate route planning and traffic adjustments.
      Emergency Planning for Environmental Hazards: Given the site’s susceptibility to landslides,
       rockfalls, and deteriorating road conditions, traffic schedules should be adaptable to respond
       to such hazards promptly. Contractors are expected to follow predetermined contingency
       routes or implement temporary closures in the event of road obstructions. In addition, signs
       that identify evacuation routes will be placed at frequent intervals in tunnels.
Responsibilities And Reporting
To ensure coordinated traffic management, clear roles and responsibilities are assigned to various
site stakeholders:
      Rogun JSC: As the primary site authority, Rogun JSC oversees overall traffic planning and
       management is responsible for monitors the effectiveness of traffic-related scheduling. Their
       drivers and vehicles also are responsible for complying with the TMP requirements that apply
       on the roads on which they travel.

       They also enforce requirements of site-wide and Contractor TMPs, including conducting
       formal inspections, making continual observations while traveling around the site, and taking

       traffic-related activities of all parties. Finally, their drivers and vehicles also are responsible

traffic management roles and responsibilities, refer to Section 6.1 Project Organisational Structure.
Transport Of Hazardous Materials, Dangerous Goods, And Waste
The transportation of hazardous materials, dangerous goods (DG), and waste at the Project site
must align with site specific protocols and applicable regulatory standards to ensure safe operations
and environmental compliance. Contractor TMPs must include appropriate requirements concerning
routes, vehicle and driver requirements.
Designated Routes And Zones
    Hazardous materials and waste must only be transported along designated routes that avoid
       high-risk areas such as pedestrian pathways and congested zones.
      Loading, unloading, and temporary staging of hazardous materials must occur in designated,
       clearly marked areas equipped with containment measures.
Vehicle And Driver Requirements
    Vehicles transporting hazardous materials and dangerous goods must meet site and
        regulatory standards, including proper labelling, containment systems, and spill response
        equipment.
      Only trained and licensed drivers approved for hazardous material transport may operate
       these vehicles.
      Minimum requirements for drivers and vehicles that carry explosives are identified in the
       Blasting and Explosives Framework. These are to be included in Contractor TMPs and
       Blasting and Explosives Plans.




PEDESTRIAN AND GROUND PERSONNEL SAFETY
PEDESTRIAN SAFETY
Pedestrian safety is a key priority at the Rogun HPP site, due to high volumes of vehicle operations
and construction activities that create significant hazards for ground personnel.
DESIGNATED PEDESTRIAN WALKWAYS AND CROSSINGS

Pedestrian walkways and crossings must be clearly defined and controlled to prevent vehicle-
pedestrian interactions. The type of separation used must be appropriate to the level of risk in each
location:
      Walkways Requiring Physical Barriers: In high-risk areas where vehicles and pedestrians
       interact frequently, such as parking zones, material storage sites, and the dam core


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                       October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                 Page | 223
       construction area, pedestrian-only walkways must be physically separated from vehicle
       routes using barriers such as guardrails, bollards, or concrete blocks.
      Walkways Where Surface Markings Are Sufficient: In lower-risk areas where vehicle speeds
       are low and traffic is controlled, clearly marked pedestrian walkways using high-visibility
       surface markings may be used instead of barriers.
      Crossings: High-visibility pedestrian crossings must be established at intersections, access
       gates, and other critical crossing points. Advance warning signage must be installed to alert
       drivers, and pedestrian right-of-way must be strictly enforced.
      Foot Traffic Outside Designated Walkways: If pedestrian movement is required outside of
       designated walkways, it should be controlled by flaggers, warning lights, or other attention-
       getting measures. Such movements should only occur if approved by a Supervisor.
All walkways and crossings must be incorporated into contractor TMPs, with layouts reviewed and
approved by the ER/PMC. These routes must be reinforced during site inductions to ensure
compliance and awareness.
MAIN SITE ACCESS GATE
The main site access gate currently lacks safe pedestrian infrastructure, creating a high risk
environment where workers on foot share roads with vehicles. The following measures must be
implemented and overseen by the Security Department in coordination with the Employer’s
Representative and PMC:
      Dedicated Pedestrian Pathways: Clearly defined, paved footpaths must be constructed
       leading from the main entrance to key site areas. Where vehicle and pedestrian interactions
       cannot be avoided, pathways must be physically separated using robust barriers such as
       concrete blocks or guardrails.
      Crossings and Visibility: High-visibility pedestrian crossings must be installed at all vehicle
       entry and exit points. Crossings must be marked with durable paint, supported by warning
       signage, and designed to give pedestrians clear right-of-way.
      Traffic and Pedestrian Separation: Physical barriers must be installed at all vehicle routes in
       high-risk locations to prevent pedestrian-vehicle interactions. Where barriers are not feasible,
       alternative traffic control measures such as flaggers or traffic signals must be used.
      Pedestrian Safety Oversight: The Security Department, in coordination with the Employer’s
       Representative and PMC, is responsible for ensuring compliance with these measures.
       Layouts at the gate must be standardised and approved by the Employer’s Representative
       and PMC to maintain site-wide consistency.
PHYSICAL BARRIERS
Physical barriers should be installed where pedestrian-vehicle interactions pose a significant safety
risk. The type of barrier used must be appropriate for the level of hazard present:
      High-Traffic and High-Risk Areas: Durable barriers such as concrete blocks, steel guardrails,
       or bollards are appropriate along haul roads, construction zones, and vehicle routes where
       pedestrian traffic is unavoidable.
      Exclusion Zones: Around high-risk construction areas, including excavation sites and loading
       zones, barriers effectively restricts access to authorised personnel only.
      Lower-Risk Areas: In locations where pedestrian traffic is infrequent, and vehicle speeds are
       controlled, clearly marked surface delineation (e.g., painted lines, signage) may serve in
       place of barriers.


Rogun Hydropower Project – Updated Environmental and Social Impact Assessment
Project No.: project number | Our Ref No.: Project REF                                      October 2025
Project Management Group, JSC “Rogun HPP”, DFZ                                                Page | 224
The Employer’s Representative, PMC, and contractors must conduct routine inspections of all
barriers to identify damage or instability. The Contractors’ TMPs will define frequency. Deficiencies
must be addressed immediately to ensure continued effectiveness.
WORKER TRANSPORTATION AND DROP-OFF/PICK-UP ZONES
Worker transit represents an important road use at the Project, with shuttle buses and other vehicles
playing a central role in transporting workers to their designated work areas, to their
accommodations, or the site gate each shift on a daily basis and on the days when 15-day shifts
change.. The following measures can enhance safety and minimise risks during such times.
DESIGNATED DROP-OFF AND PICK-UP ZONES

Drop-off and pick-up zones must be strategically planned in coordination with the Traffic Department
to prioritise worker safety and reduce interactions with moving vehicles. Some locations will be
defined by ER/PMC, while others will be determined by contractors based on site-specific risks. \
      Strategic Placement: Drop-off and pick-up zones must be located in designated low-risk
       areas, including near the main gate and worker accommodations, away from high-traffic
       routes, intersections, and active construction zones.
      Safety Features: All zones must be equipped with clear signage, painted road markings to
       delineate pedestrian and vehicle areas, sufficient lighting, and robust physical barriers such
       as fencing or bollards to separate pedestrians from vehicle movement.
      Tunnel-Specific Safety: Where pick-up and drop-off zones are required in tunnels, they must
       be physically demarcated and include adequate lighting to ensure visibility.
      TMP Integration: Contractors must document their designated pick-up and drop-off zone
       layouts in their TMPs, with review and approval by the ER/PMC to ensure alignment with
       site-wide safety standards.
      Worker Training. Contractors must train their workers to remain in pedestrian-safe areas and
       not to travel around the site on foot. This should be subject to sanctions if workers continue
       to disregard these rules.
SHUTTLE BUS SERVICE REQUIREMENTS

A formalised shuttle bus service would help to minimise congestion, improve road safety, and
ensure timely transport for workers. The ER/PMC and Rogun JSC should evaluate the need for
such a service. If they determine that such a service should be implemented, the ER/PMC will work
with Contractors to design the program, which would then be under the joint management of the
ER/JSC and the Traffic Department, in consultation with Rogun JSC. Once in place, Contractors
would be responsible for ensuring that their workforce uses the designated service while on site
rather than personal or ad hoc transport arrangements.
Scheduled Services:
    Shuttle buses should operate on a fixed schedule with a frequency of 15–30 minutes,
       particularly during peak demand periods, particularly during shift changes.
      Additional buses should be provided during the twice-monthly shift changeover days to
       accommodate the large influx and departure of workers.
Collaboration Among Contractors:
Contractors should use the centralised shuttle service rather than operating independent transport
services unless alternative arrangements are approved by the ER/PMC.

Designated Bus Stops:
Formal bus stops should be established at key locations, wThe following initial locations are
suggested:
      Site Entrance Gate
      At least one location in Rogun City
      Accommodation Blocks
      Construction Area 1

      Contractor Offices
All bus stops should be equipped with the following infrastructure to ensure worker safety during
boarding and alighting:
      Clear signage indicating designated boarding and drop-off zones.
      Adequate lighting to ensure visibility, particularly for night shifts.
      Protective barriers or safe standing areas to shield workers from passing traffic.
Mandatory Shuttle Bus Safety Features:

In addition to the requirements that apply to all site vehicles (refer to section 8), shuttle busses
should meet the following requirements:
      Steps and handrails: Steps should be fully deployed and handrails secure before workers
       board or alight.
      Lighting: Interior and exterior lights should be functional for visibility during early morning and
       night-time operations.
      Seat belts: All buses should be equipped with functional seat belts for every passenger, and
       seat belt use must be enforced.
      Emergency exits and safety features: Emergency exits should be unobstructed, clearly
       marked, and in working condition.
BUS DRIVER TRAINING

Drivers should complete additional training that includes:
      Safe navigation of designated routes and adherence to schedules.
      Compliance with safety protocols at pick-up/drop-off zones.
      Procedures for managing peak times and ensuring safe boarding and alighting.
ONGOING REVIEW AND ADJUSTMENTS
Contractor supervisors, in coordination with the ER/PMC , should periodically evaluate shuttle
operations to ensure they meet site demands and adjust as necessary:
      Capacity Reviews: Assess the need for additional buses, increased trip frequency, or new
       pick-up/drop-off locations during peak times or as site activities evolve.
      Risk Assessments: All new or adjusted locations should be verified to meet the safety criteria
       outlined previously.

PERSONAL PROTECTIVE EQUIPMENT (PPE)
Ground personnel must wear high-visibility PPE, including reflective vests and hard hats, at all times
to ensure visibility to vehicle operators.
Non-compliance with PPE requirements will be treated as a safety violation and must be addressed
immediately by contractors in accordance with the disciplinary procedures outlined in the OHS
Management Plan.
PPE compliance must be reinforced through contractor inductions, supervisor inspections, and
ER/PMC site monitoring to ensure consistent adherence to safety standards.
PEDESTRIAN SAFETY AWARENESS AND INDUCTIONS
Contractors must ensure pedestrian safety is a core component of induction training and ongoing
awareness initiatives to reinforce safe practices and compliance across the site:
      Induction Training: Initial site inductions must include guidance on designated pedestrian
       pathways, crossing rules, and mandatory PPE requirements.
      Toolbox Talks: Conduct regular toolbox talks to reinforce safe pedestrian behaviours,
       address site-specific risks, and promote adherence to safety protocols.
      Awareness Campaigns: Implement periodic campaigns to improve understanding and
       compliance with pedestrian safety measures, using visuals, posters, and briefings to engage
       workers effectively.
For detailed training and induction requirements related to traffic and road safety, refer to Section
15.


SECURITY AND TRAFFIC COORDINATION
INTEGRATION OF SECURITY AND TRAFFIC OPERATIONS
The Rogun JSC Security Department manages both site security and traffic operations, ensuring
these functions work together to maintain controlled access and safe vehicle movement.
ALIGNMENT WITH THE SECURITY MANAGEMENT PLAN
The Rogun HPP Security Management Plan (Volume 3, A7 of this ESIA) outlines site-specific
security protocols, roles, and infrastructure requirements. This TMP has been developed to align
with the Security Management Plan.
The Security Department, ER/PMC, and contractors must ensure that access control measures,
vehicle monitoring, and enforcement mechanisms outlined in the Security Management Plan do not
conflict with relevant requirements of the OHS Management Plan. Any updates to security protocols
that impact traffic operations must be reviewed jointly to maintain consistency across all site safety
frameworks.
